Tales of the Riverine.
“Dictionary Nod.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
BY PRIUE WARUNG.
Emerson says soiu.e where that it is an
achievement of high eloquence to confer an ex-
pressive nick-name, but no particularly fine
ouality, oratorical or other, was needed to attach
n bye-name to Ned .
Ah— now I’m stuck ! It has never dawned
upon me till this moment that in all the years
t liat I knew Ned to bo spoken of, I never heard
his surname once. Perhaps none of the men who
referred to him constantly as one of the biggest
“cures” in that region of “cures” and “queer
cards,” the Riverine district —where each square
mile has its tale of some human soul going to
wreck in its sea of grey plain—knew his surname.
Perhaps he hadn’t one to know. Most likely,
indeed, was this the case. His earliest recollection
went back to the time when he was tending
Parson Marsden’s black poleys on the venerable
Principal Chaplain’s grant at Bathurst, so, as
likely as not, Ned was one of the hundreds of
infants that were cradled in Parramatta Factory
and sent into the world worse than motherless and
with a choice among 50 fathers. At all events, I
am pretty sure none of the fellows who spoke of
y ß d to me knew his surname. Else it must
have slipped out sometimes. But it was always
‘•Dictionary Ned.”
Xow I was saying that anybody without the
faintest touch of eloquence could have given Ned
tliat name—would iiave conferred no other upon
il,im had the selection of a hundred appellations
been offered. Even a Victorian shire-councillor,
who is the least oratorical person in existence,
except when he wants a railway to his front gate,
would have called him “ Dictionary Ned.” The
cjngruity of the term was so obvious that a blind
man could have seen it.
The name had its origin in the fact that Ned
always carried a dictionary-a wonderful sort of
one. It was based “ upon the labours of Johnson,
Worcester, Webster,” and Heaven knows how
many more, “ and incorporated the latest results
uf the most modern and scientific Lexico-
graphers.” Further, it was illustrated by “ one
thousand Superb Engravings from drawings by the
Best Artists’ (I am quoting from the title page)
—and as though that was not enough to furnish
to the humble student, it supplied, at the fore-
part, an “Analytical History of the Growth
of the English Language (in two pages), and, in
appendices, a ‘Glossary’ of Scientific Terms, a
Classical Encyclopedia, and a Collection of Pro-
verbial Sayings and Phrases from all Languages.”
The whole of this overflowing repository of learn-
ing had been published at sixpence; and Ned
paid ninepence for it on his solitary trip to
Melbourne at Cole’s bookstall in Paddy's Market,
in Bourke-street, 25 years ago. Cole wasn’t a
millionaire then, and you couldn’t buy books at
English prices. You paid ninepence for a sixpenny
publication, and fifteen-pence for a shilling one,
andhalf-a-crown, and sometimes three shillings, for
a two-shilling volume. And Ned, paying nine-
pence, had therewith purchased a treasure of
wealth untellable, of joys limitless. All the
glorious orbs in the firmament of culture swam
into his ken when he pocketed the book.
Keats never extracted from Chapman’s Homer,
Landor from Shakespeare, Lindsey Gordon from
Horace, Marcus Clarke from Balzac, one thou-
sandth part of the delight Ned obtained from his
dictionary. The only volume ho ever possessed, to
him it was a library, a literature, mauy-volumed
life itself. Somehow or other, the intangible
charm that the mere study of words as words, as
the fossils of human experience and emotion, has
for so many people, had entranced this illiterate
wanderer whose home was always on the fringe
of “settled country,” who was never at ease
except he was “ inside,” who got astray in a one
street township and was utterly lost in the soli-
tudes of a great city. Amongst the thousand
and one types of humanity that wandered
over the Old Man and One Tree Plains, there was
the man who never turned in till ho had read a
chapter out of his Bible, there was the man who
carried a shilling Shakespeare, and the other who
kept his mind alive on Byron, there was the fellow
who always kept a woman’s photograph, and the
one who nourished his soul amid deadening wastes
by the crucifix dangling round his neck. The
consolation that each and all derived from his and
their several idols, Ned drank in from his pocket-
dictionary.
lie had bought it with the vain hope of
making up the deficiencies of his early educa-
tion. “Never too late to larn, boys,” he would
assure the scoffers. And though he was wise in
the wondrous lore of the plains and the rive s, “up
to any dodge ” in free-selection and station-life,
knew every trick of bush-craft and of river-craft,
had overlanded cattle in the early gold days from
Adelaide to Forest Creek, and (earlier still) sheep
from Bathurst to the valley of the Wannon in far
western “ Port Phillip,” and had held the wheel
of the Lady Augusta when she ran up to Tocum-
wal months before Cadell made his first chart of
the Murray, he esteemed his precious knowledge,
out of which so many “smarter” men had coined
small fortunes, to be worthless compared with
“book-laming.” At every interval of leisure,
and during the times of work when the duty of
the moment would not be impeded by “study,”
out would come his book. When other men
smoked or swopped yarns, or drank, Ned studied.
There was nothing complex about his methods.
1 le went straight at the business of mastering its
contents.
Somewhere about forty years of age when he
invested his ninepence, by the time he became
bargeman of the Royal Duke barge—she was built
in the epoch when ferocious “loyalty” dislocated
a man’s neck as consort of the Currency La>s
steamer, he was within a year or two of fifty. And
unremitting in his pursuit of knowledge with all
sorts of difficulties, he had mastered the dictionary
so far as “ V,” the page that begins with “ vital ”
and ends with “ votive.” Ten words a day for a
minimum, and a hundred for a maximum—when
he had a rare holiday he totted up the hundred —
he had got off by rote the words and their mean-
ings. And this was without counting the
Sundays. He had devoted those to the “ Glossary”
and the, “ Proverbial Sayings and Phrases,’ and
to the horrible combination of letters which Ih >
dictionary-compiler, on the authority of the
modern and scientific lexicographer, used to
express the pronunciation of the foreign phrases.
It was a gigantic task this last, and Ned had done
well to devote his Sabbaths to it when he had no
other occupation than to boil his white shirt for
the next Sunday. Mezzofanti himself would have
felt the task of memorising the phrases to be
herculean if lie had also found it necessary to
recjlh-et the kev to their pronunciation. .lust
think of the labour of getting off by heart not
merely the interpretation, for instance, of
L’homme propose el Dieu dispose, but the weird
and mysterious conjunction of letters which the
latest modern and scientific lexicographer
expressed the sound of the phrase: “lom-pro-a-
dyoo-dis-poz.” But all these fearful and wonder-
ful shibboleths were gradually being mastered by
Ned—to an accent, lie was a beggar to learn,
was Ned. As he himself said, “he warn’t no
slouch at stickin’ to it.”
That was the curious thing about Ned. All Ins
grinding away at vocabularies never seemed to
tinge by the faintest degree of refined colour his
vigorous colloqualism. Perhaps, in some distaiit
period, when he contemplated sitting under his
own vine and fig-tree —the most errant knight of
the Murrumbidgee plains has always a vision of
some roof-tree o’ his ain—he intended to employ
his “dictionary words,” but in the days of his
scholarship he kept to the rude, energetic speech
of his kind.
Nor was it clear why he hadn’t begun “laming”
before he reached middle-age. Once he was
interrogated upon the point, but the answer,
though apparently at the moment conclusive, does
not appear a sufficient explanation now that we
can look back.
11.
It was in the following circumstances that the
question was put to him, and the answer re-
ceived. Ned was running shares in a fencing
contract on Benduck at the ’Bidgee. Sunday
came, and Ned, having washed and ironed the
white shirt he had worn at intervals during the
week in readiness for the succeeding Sunday,
donned his other “ b’iled rag ” and the moleskins
which lie called his “ go-to-meetln’s,” though they
never went there. His toilette operations weie
observed with keen interest by one of the fellows
who had sundowned it to the men’s hut the
previous night in the expectation of the plum-duff
and lot of rum which Benduck, even more liberal
than other stations, invariably gave to the Satur-
day to Monday “whalers.” A man of middle
height and naturally sturdy frame, already, while
still young, he carried himself with the swagman’s
stoop, and his watery eyes gave the lie to the story
of natural or acquired refinement which the clear-
cut features would have otherwise told. Dirty
with the indescribable dirt of the man of position
who is debased to a congenial gutter, College Bill
revolted, as he lay on a sheepskin in the early sun-
shine, against Ned’s sacrifice at the altar of clean-
“ Who’s that toff ? ” he asked of the cook.
“bin? W’y, that’s Dic-shun-cry Ned ! Never
’erd of ’im ? ”
“ That Dictionary Ned ! My word, I didn't
know he went in for white shirts and collars ! I’ll
have some fun with him, boys—just you come and
watch ! ” .
Now, College Bill was esteemed a bit of a
humourist, or rather of a satirist. For a “Bishop
Barker” he would compose a quatrain on any
subject—a person preferred—suggested by the man
who tipped him the drink, ana for a bottle of
brandy he would write a fifty-line satire and recite
it with becoming action. It was whispered among
the bosses and “colonial-experiencers ” that he
was a University prize poem man, but that could
hardly be so, for Bill’s “Ode to Swipes,” the
shanty-keeper at One Tree, was quoted all over the
Riverine for its originality and force, and since
the first University was, there has never been a
prize-poem which contained these qualities.
Being what he was. Bill always had a claque,
and when he meandered his odorous carcase to the
tank side, where Ned was performing his toilette,
lie was followed by several others of the sundown-
ing brotherhood prepared in advance to applaud
his satirical efforts at old Dictionary’s expense.
They were already in laughter by the time they
had covered half the distance between the hut and
the tank, for Bill, as ho walked, improvised—
Devoted student! while other “ whalers” slumber
He studies hard, does Dictionary Ned !
But still he’s storing only useless lumber
In the squash pumpkin which he calls his head 1
“You’re Dictionary Ned, arn’t you?” began
Bill, as he approached. “I’m Bill College
Bill, the boys call me. Shake !”
Ned turned at the remark. Perhaps it was the
circumstance that his fingers were busy buttoning
his collar—he had only the one collar—that pre-
vented him accepting Bill’s preferred hand. Any-
how, he did not take it. He simply nodded and
said :
“Oh, yer Bill, are yer ?—College Bill? I’ve
hern o’ yer 1”
“Yes,” responded Bill, not feeling quite so easy
as he should, his reception was so chilling, “ no
doubt you have. Being brother students, y’
know ” —he paused to wink impressively at his
admirers —“ we ought to be acquainted 1”
“Ye-es —yer think so?” drawled Ned.
‘ ‘ Of course. Men of culture are not too numerous
on the river country.”
“So?”
There was a titter, not quite at Ned’s expense.
Bill was annoyed at it, and grew truculent.
“ Do you know 1 think you should drop the
‘ Ned,’ my friend.”
“Yes?”
“ You should style yourself ‘Dick.’ ‘Dictionary
Dick ’ would be not only alliterative, but would
be more euphonious !” The chorus of laughter
again went up—now on Bill’s side. Wasn’t Bill
rubbing it in ! What jaw-breakers lie could use !
Ned didn't reply, but withdrew his dictionary
from the hip-pocket where he always carried it.
“ Going to look up ‘ alliterative ’ and
‘euphonious,’ old chap?” Bill continued. “Now
don’t turn up ‘ H —you’ll find the first under ‘A,’
and ‘euphonious’ under ‘E.’” And the rouse-
abouts and swagmen grew merrier. Bill was
getting on well with his chiacking of old Ned !
Ned, however, was still silent.
“Ned,” went on Bill, “ the only two men I ever
heard of that were books in breeches were
Macaulay and yourself. Macaulay was a beggar
to talk, but you’re a beggar to keep your mouth
shut. Oh, and by-the-way, why didn’t you take
your schooling earlier ?”
“ I don’t usu’lly ring my clapper on’y when I’ve
got some toon to play,” now responded Ned.
“ That answers yer first remark. An’, as ter the
second, it’s my turn ter ask questions. Yer can
read French, Mister Bill Boozer—l beg parding—
I mean. Mister College Bill ?”
Over his drunkard’s rosiness, Bill’s face showed
a deeper red. And the audience rejoiced exceed-
ingly. “Who’d ’a thought as ol’ Dicshunery
war goin’ ter take down College Bill ?”
“Can you read French, Mister? I’ve hear say
that yer ses as yer could. An’ if yer can, jest rip
out that for th’ pres’nt comp’ny 1” He held out
in one hand the dictionary open at the “Appendix
of Foreign Phrases,” and with his other fore-
finger pointed out the phrases wdiich he wished
interpreted. Bill stared, and the carmine of his
dissoluteness and of his shame vanished together.
Grey-green, like the salt-bush scrub near by, was
his quivering face.
“Look y’ere, coves,” went on Ned. “He’s a
college chap, an’ can’t tell yer what tnau— mo,

“ As he can’t or won’t, coves, I’ll tell yer? ‘ Mo-

ses, an’ a bad subject is a no-good-sort-o’-chap, a

should like ter know ? Ain’t that Bill’s picter —

ter chiack me, the poor, ignorant roustabout as is

An’ what’s he done wi’ it now he’s ’ad it ? Used

prove that Gawd Almighty didn’t know His own

his speech lengthened, and, with his final words,
raised his right hand as though in defiance of the
Supreme Wisdom.
“ Wot d’yer mean, Dicshinnery ?” asked one of
the group.
“What do I mean? Here Gawd gives that
boozer, that cove what’ll sell his lamin’ for a ball
—I forgot as yet fellows call it a shout now-a-days—
an’ his soul for a bottle o’ the stuff, Gawd, I say,
gives him—him, the swine—’Varsity eddication,
an’ me, an’ some o’ yer as well, who thirsts for it,
and hungers for it, why, He don’t give us our
ABC !”
Bill quailed beneath the artillery of scorn that
all eyes now levelled upon him, and walked away,
In that brief space, he had lost one nickname and
gained a choice of two others. He was no longer
Gollege Bill, but ever after, according to his com-
pany, “God Almighty’s Mistake,’ # and “ mo-ya-
soo-jay.” More French was spoken in Riverina
in consequence of this episode than was taught
in its schools. And the teacher was simple
“ Dictionary Ned ” and the manual, his ninepenny
compilation of the lexicographers’ labours.
HI-
A man’s hobbv is oftentimes his death, as well
as his delight. This was Ned’s case.
The great spurt of pastoral enterprise in
Southern Riverina, which occurred in the late
Sixties, operated advantageously upon Ned, as
it did on everybody else except the shepherds who
found themselves being gradually displaced by
“Hylands’’and “ Whitecross,” and the old-style
squatter who wouldn’t move with the times, and
who consequently got “shifted”into back country
and Queer-street in one and the same impulse,
Ned, when the spurt set in, gave up rouseabout
and odd job work, and took finally to the rivers,
One short season as deckhand proved his trust-
worthiness, and the next season he was promoted
to bargeman. Ten pound three and fourpence per
month, live like a fighting-cock—there never was
a boat on the rivers which kept its hands on
skimpv allowance, save one—and the 1 est chance,
if he wished it, to ullage the cargo; this was his
billet. Too honest, however, to ullage, accustomed
to live too sparely to revel in luxury, Ned seem-
ingly put his increased screw to no better purpose
than the rebinding of his dictionary.
The cUth cover of tin treasured volume had
become d'lapidated, an 1 one day when the
Currency Lass was mooie.l at Echuca wharf, ho
took advantage of a “smoke-oh ! ” spell to run up
to the Riverine Herald office, then managed by
Johnny D for Angus Mackay, of Bendigo,
“ Yer can get this re-bound for n.e, Johnny—fine
now, an’gilt edges.” “ I’ll send it to Melbourne,”
said Johnny, gingerly turning over the greasy,
thumb-marked pages, “to Detmold.” “Butdon’t
you think, Ned, it’s time you had a new copy ?’
‘ A new copy !” echoed Ned. “ It’s easy ter see
as yer not a leadin’ man, Mr. D , for all yer
run a noospaper. If yer were, ye’d know as a
book a lone man ’as used for a matter of ten years
is mother, an’ missus, an’ kids, an’drink, an’ all
ter him ! A new copy ! Blazes !” And so the
old book round which the tendrils of Ned’s heart
had grown, went down to Detmold, the famed
Melbourne binder, to be dressed in delioately-per-
fumed leather, with flexible sides, and riehly-
tooled bands, and gloriously-gilt edges, and all the
rest of the finery so delightful to the book-lover’s
mind and so ruinous to his purse,
During the month following Ned felt all the
distraction and all the sorrows that spring from a
gap made in one’s life when his hobby-horse is
stolen from him. No man gets half the pleasure
out of his business that he does out of his hobby,
and he bears, therefore, the loss of his business
better than he does that of the avocation of his
leisure. And Ned was disconsolate, and would
not be comforted—“moped,” as his mate said,
“like a native companion, and sorrowed as Jim
Dickson, of the Cumberooraa, didn’t when he
became a widder-man.” It was a standing joke
on the rivers how Dickson had sorrowed when the
news of his wife’s death reached him from Sydney,
He was at Hay, and he hired the galvanised-iron
Temperance-hall for a dance that same night.
“ I’ve got to dissipate my grief, ladies and gents!”
he said to his guests. And if the depth of his
sadness is to be gauged by the extent of his dissi-
pation, he was pre-eminent in grief amongst men.
Ned, however, didn't drown his sorrow in Lind-
say’s beer. He simply moped,
But as the time drew near when he might expect
the book, he brightened up. The Currency Lass,
with her somewhat disreputable consort, the Royal
Duke, was coming down stream from Burrabogie
station to top up at Hay with back-block clip,
when, just in the narrow bend opposite the town
cemetery, the crafts met the Resolution and
barge laboriously steaming with timber and wire
for the up-river runs. The warning-signals were
exchanged and the steamers were slowed down.
It was an awkward place to meet in. It takes
careful steering enough to allow for the tangental
force of the current in a sharp bend m carrying a
steamer round the curves ; it is trebly difficult
when it is a question of getting a barge round as
well; but the task became formidable when the
surge and sweep of the current is complicated with
the rule of the road and two not-too-smartly-
handled boats going in the opposite direction.
Still, all would have gone well if it had not been
for the Dictionary.
Linton, skipper of the Resolution, was at her
wheel. On recognising the Currency Lass ahead
he had sent down to his cabin for a parcel which
had been entrusted to his care, and when he saw
Ned, as he expected, at the wheel of the Lass’
barge, ho hailed him, and brought the Resolution’s
stern a point nearer the slowly-moving Royal
Duke. •
“Ned, there! Diction-ary Ned !”
“Aye, aye, Mr. Linton !’’
“ ’Speoting anything, Ned ?”
AH Ned’s desolation and expectation went out
in his eager tones.
“Yes, skipper! My book—’ave yer got it?”
In his excitement he had forgotten his usual
cautious devotion to the work in hand. Instead
of keeping his barge’s head inshore he steadied it
mechanically.
“ Yes !” shouted Linton, in reply. “Johnny
D said you’d growl like h if it was
knocked about in the mail, and as I was first boat
he asked me to bring it. Here you are!”
Holding his wheel with his left hand, he drew
with his right the carefully-wrapped package from
the seat of the pilot-house, and swung his arm so
as to gain impetus for the throw.
“Ready, Ned,” he called ; “Lookout!” He
did not notice that in the movement he had
shifted the Resolution’s course dangerously near
the Royal Duke. Nor did Ned, fearful the book
might fall in mid-stream, perceive his duty. He
shouted to Linton, “Hold hard, skipper!” and
ran along the wheel-platform so that the toss
would be an easy one.
On river barges the wheel-stand is shifted with
the disposition of the cargo-now for’ard, now
aft; now raised, now lowered. On long trips it is
geared ; on short ones, held in position simply by
its own weight and the rudder-chains. Now,
from Burrabogie to Hay was but a few hours’
trip, and as the top-bales would have to here-
stowed at the township wharf, the platform of
the Duke was not stayed. Running to its flat
end to get his precious book, Ned forgot this.
When he remembered it, he was between the
sheathing of the Duke and the steamer—jammed !
A crunch, and a shriek, and a horrid splash !
In their beginnings all catastrophes are so
simple—are so easily preventive if the initial
blunder in the man or the weakness in the thing
could be only understood to be the beginning of
disaster! The gearing of that wheel-platform
to the vessel’s sides—and it would not have
slipped under Ned’s rush to port? A turn of her
wheel, and the Resolution would have stood up-
stream ten feet away from the barge. But the
platform of bolted planks was not stayed down,
Linton’s hand failed to send the gaily-painted
spokes revolving, and the result was that poor old
Dictionary was done for. It was not a very
heroic way of dying, perhaps—and for the sake
of a ninepenny book, too ! But, then, the book
was Ned’s hobby, and men go to the death, do they
not, all the world over for their hobbies. A king
is but a hobby, a woman another ; and Ned’s hobby
had never deceived him—had never smitten him
with the despair of broken faith! And of what
king or woman can that be said?
IY.
He lingered for a few days, sometimes partly
conscious, sometimes delirious —oftener still,
pathetically dead to sound, almost to sight. But
about ten hours before his death—he “went
inside ” at sun-down on Sunday—he revived to a
calm clearness of brain. Among the watchers—
there were as many as the hospital surgeon would
permit—was one whom Ned was vainly trying to
recognise. And the man saw the glance and
understood.
“ Don’t you know me, ‘Dictionary?’ I’m Bill
—College Bill! The fellow you gave it to so hotly
on Ben duck three or four years ago—don’t you
remember ? ”
A flicker of a surprised smile shone on Ned’s
face, whose tan was changing to pallor.

“ In-cin-er-ate—to burn to ashes.’’

“ Wait! What folks do with their corpsuses-
when they don’t want ter —be buried ? ” Strange

“Order their bodies to be given to the hospi-

“That's the word ! Lor’, what a thing 'tis ter
have lamin’, Bill! But the word ain’t in any

“ Hang a will! Bill’s ter have all the spons -

And he mumbled on and on, into slumber— and
at sundown he went “ beyond the boundary ”
* # *
Boyd did fix it—very irregularly, and to
the detriment of the Curator of Intestate Estates’
Commission —as soon as he was satisfied that Bill’s
claim was bond-Jidc. And it is gratifying to know
that Ned’s few hundreds planted his legatee’s feet
firmly on the upward path. Bill has suffered in
all these years only one relapse from decent
behaviour. That was when, in 77, he stood for
Parliament. But, by that time, be had won
popular respect, and people thought too much of
him to give him ids way. They rejected him, and
he is still, therefore, an honoured member of the
community, and unqualified for gaol.
And as v to the manner of the incineration of
Bargeman Ned and his dictionary—why, that is a
tale for another time.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 3, 1892.
The Queensland Exodus.
The grotesque scheme of wholesale emigra-
tion to the Argentine States, lately formu-
lated by some feather-brained leaders of the
Queensland unions, is gradually assuming a
concrete form. When this amazing project
was first advanced The Bulletin regarded
it as so utterly foolish and impracticable, so
hopeless, and so glaringly ridiculous as to be
hardly worth serious comment. It was
difficult to believe that any sane body of
men could actually entertain such an idea ; it
seemed an amusing chimera, and nothing
more. But according to recent advices it
would appear that the exodus is actually to
be attempted. Sundry delegates were
despatched to the Argentine States, and
their mission appears to have met with some
measure of success. An immense grant of
land is said to have been secured ; and unless
something of an unexpected nature should
intervene the very best and most enterprising
members of the Queensland democracy will
shortly undertake the hopeless task of con-
structing an ideal commonwealth on the
dreary plains of La Plata. It is almost in-
variably the bone and muscle of the nation
which goes forth on such expeditions—in
other words, it is the strongest section of the
community—the section which should have
led the way in restoring freedom to Queens-
land which proposes to be the first to give
up the contest and retreat ignominiously,
leaving the Kanaka Cabinet masters of the
situation.
• * *
The one aspect of the case which cannot bo
disguised is that the proposed exodus to the
Argentine is a confession of defeat. For about
three years the Queensland labour-unions
have carried on a desultory warfare with the
Griffith Government, and now they are
apparently weary of the contest, and those
who can escape are anxious to retire from
the battlefield. Most of the leaders are
going, and the followers who cannot go are
to be left in the lurch. Three years—for it
is only during that period that the struggle
with a retrogressive Government has assumed
an acute form—is not so very long ; many
races which the Australian regards with pro-
found contempt have maintained a far harder
jontlict for three centuries, and though their
enfranchisement is to-day almost as remote
as over, they show no signs of giving up the
struggle. There has been no effort to upset
the incubus of the Coalition, either by con-
stitutional means or by force and bloodshed,
and the unionist leaders have not the excuse
that they have staked their all in the cause
of Liberty and lost. They have staked nothing
as yet, and lost nothing ; in fact, they pro-
pose to fly before the battle has seriously
commenced. There has been no general
election in Queensland since Griffith’s in-
sanity came upon him, and he set up in
business as a new description of absolute
monarch ; and, therefore, the democracy
has not even definitely joined issue with
its enemies. Its strength is an unknown
quantity ; even if it makes its best
effort at the polls it may be defeated, in
which case the Kanaka party has a fresh
lease of existence for another three years,
and there is no immediate hope of improve-
ment ; on the other hand, it may achieve
such a triumph that—provided it makes good
use of its victory—Queensland will belong to
it for ever afterwards. But until the effort
is made, any attempt at wholesale emigration
is treason to, and base desertion of, the cause
of Liberty. The prominent unionists who
are leading the movement were appointed to
the office they hold in order that they might
do their best to save Australia from the curse
of capitalism—not that they might bolt igno-
miniously with a few of their followers, and
found a Gtopia in Argentina. Their exodus
will appreciably weaken the democratic vote;
it will dishearten the democracy by proving
conclusively that its chiefs have no faith in
themselves or in their own cause ; and it will
play the game of the Gkiffilwraith Coalition
as effectually as even the Coalition’s paid
hirelings could have done.
« # #
Moreover, even assuming that the Queens-
land argonauts owe no duty whatever to their
country and their cause —that there is no
ignominy in their hurried flight to escape
from one fat capitalist and one lean one—
that the leaders were appointed for the ex-
press purpose that they might desert the
bulk of their followers at the earliest oppor-
tunity and provide for themselves in the
green (because distant) pastures of Argentina
—there are other aspects of the case. The
movement is only another instance of that
eternal craving to move on which is the
Anglo-Saxon’s panacea for every ill. He is
haunted by a conviction that somewhere on
this round earth there is a terrestrial Paradise
and if he only keeps moving long enough he
will find it ; and the one lesson which he will
not learn is that there is no such Paradise to
be found even if he moves from the rising to
the setting of the sun, and from the wastes
beneath the Pole star to the Antarctic zone.
The writer of this has travelled in many
lands, and Australia—bad as it may be—is
the best of them all. Which does not say
much for this continent, but it says still less
for the rest of the globe. There is no single
advantage to be found in La Plata which
could not be found equally well in West
Australia, in Tasmania, in Maoriland, or
anywhere in the vicinity of Rockhampton, or
of Gosford in New South Wales, or in a
thousand other localities equally familiar.
Its one recommendation is its remoteness ;
it is so remote that very few of the deluded
emigrants will ever get back.
« * *
The leading attraction of the promised
land that lies beside the Rio de la Plata is
the concession which, wo are told, has been
made on so lavish a scale by the Argentine
Government. But, even assuming that this
grant is absolutely reliable—which is doubt-
ful —that it is not liable to be revoked by any
future Government, or abolished in some
future revolution, the cost of reaching the
new labour commonwealth is so heavy that
the result will bo much the same as if the
emigrants bought the site of their great
experiment from the Queensland Lands
Office, and remained at home to over-
turn the Coalition at next general election.
The land, at the best, is probably some
drought-cursed tract on the remote pampas
where all the worst features ot Queensland
scenery are reproduced with rare fidelity. It
is difficult to imagine that the Argentine
Government, any more than that of Queens-
land, would give away a whole province of
good agricultural or pastoral territory gratis
to any casual strangers who asked for it-
especially as the Treasury at Buenos Ayres
is at the present moment ten times more in-
solvent than even that at Brisbane. There-
fore the probabilities are either that the
alleged grant is situated in some utterly
unsaleable desert, or that it lies in some
border region where the Australian colonists
will come in handy as a buffer in case of in-
vasion, or that the State intends to get the
value of its concession in some way out of
the colonists at some future date, or else that
there is no grant at all. And evtn if all
these doubts are satisfactorily settled the
Argentine presents few attractions. The
Queensland emigrants want to getaway from
a country which is ruined by misgovernment
and a great commercial crisis, and they pro-
pose to go to a land that is still more
ruinously misgoverned, and where the com-
mercial crisis is far more severe. Also, they
want to escape from cheap Kanaka, Chinese,
Italian, and other pauper labour, and
to do so they contemplate going where
pauper labour is seen almost at its worst.
There are over 1| million Italians in Argen-
tina and much other assorted cheap labour
of almost all the nationalities on earth, com-
pand to which the Kanaka and the other
low-priced intruders of Queensland are too
few to be worth counting. The wealth of
the country, per head of population, is less
than one-third that of Australia. Ihe
Government lias all the evil charac-
teristics of Griliilwraithism with many
others peculiarly its own. It is reck-
less, utterly corrupt, and aggravated by
occasional dictatorships and revolutions, and
the nation is loaded down with useless mili-
tary burdens, and subject to intervals of
martial law and administration at the cannon’s
mouth. It is a land, in short, where the
strong military adventurer comes uppermost
—a troubled land, full of broken banks and
depreciated paper-money and starving “un-
employed,” and all manner of disasters.
• # »
Last of all, the moral aspect of the case is
worthy of consideration. The Queensland
refugees propose to abandon Australia be-
cause it is ruined by land-grabbing and
capitalism, which are contrary to all demo-
cratic principles, and, therefore, it is un-
seemly that these same refugees should
straightway go land - grabbing in South
America on their own account. For the
men who object to the Chinaman, or the
-British capitalist, or Thomas MTlwraith’s
foreign syndicates invading their own country
and seizing on its fertile acres, to go on an
exactly similar errand to a foreign land, is
base hypocrisy. The patriots who raise the
cry, “Australia for the Australians !” should
recognise that Argentina should also be for
the Argentines. The race whose hope is in
the future independence of our rich and
wide-spreading land, should stay to win that
independence for itself. The people of La
Plata gained their freedom many years
ago by a desperate struggle against
heavy odds, and if the Australian has
not the patience and the energy to
carve out his own destiny in like manner
at least he should not endeavour to reap
what others have sown. The people of this
continent rose up in a memorable outburst
of wrath when William Booth proposed to
plant a semi-socialistic colony of British
paupers in Australia, and begged in the
name of charity for a tract of land on which
to conduct his experiment, and it is a strange
appendix to that episode that almost at the
same moment a colony of Australians should
begin semi-pauper fashion for-a similar con-
cession from a Spanish-American State. It
would be interesting to know if the parallel
is likely to go any further. If Booth had
obtained his grant the title-deeds would
have been made out in his own name, and
he could thereupon have abandoned the
cause of charity and set up in business as a
rack-renting landlord, had he been so
inclined. In whose name will the Argentine
grant be made out, and what security will
the colonists have that their trustee will
keep faith with them ? Also, is there any
visible reason to believe that the Argentine
Government will keep better faith with the
Queensland emigrants than the Queensland
Government has done with the Italians,
Kanakas, and others whom it lured under
various pretences to the shores of Northern
Australia ? And if the whole Argentine
scheme proves the bubble which it appears
to be, will the cheap Australian stranded in
La Plata be entitled to any more sympathy
than the cheap Chinaman or Italian stranded
in Australia '( These are questions which
urgently want answering, and the answers
are dilhcult to find.
Labouchere and Queen Victoria.
This exclusion of Hunky Labouciiere from
(Gladstone’s Cabinet is the only really signi-
ficant feature connected with the composi-
tion of that spurious “Liberal” body. How
significant the incident is, may be understood
by the commotion which it has occasioned. The
Tory newspapers are everywhere commenting
with undisguised delight on the matter, and
affect to sneer at Labouchere’s pretensions.
These sneers are absurdly forced. It has not
been pretended that it would have been ridicu-
lous for any single one of the persons whom
Gladstone hats included in his Administration
to have expected such selection. Yet, intellec-
tually and politically, the personality of
Labouciiere towers above that of every mem-
ber of the Cabinet, with the doubtful excep-
tions of John Morley, Sir William Harcourt
and Lord Rosebery. No candid man can really
conceal from himself that the reason for
Labouchere’s exclusion has not been that he
is too insignificant, but that he is too signifi-
cant ; not that lie is too small, but that lie is
too big. Further, for years past, Labouciiere
has been indicated as a certain Minister of
State should a Liberal Cabinet assume office,
and during all that time there have been no
sneers at the idea, although there have been
many cries of repugnance and of fear from
Tory quarters. That his qualifications for office
were recognised, has been amply demonstrated
by the fact, stated by himself and still un-
challenged, that he had been invited to write
declining inclusion in the Administration. This
proceeding furnished clear evidence that Mr.
Gladstone perceived that he would be com-
promised alike by appointing Mr. Labouciiere
to a portfolio and by omitting to offer him one,
and wanted him to help him out of the dilemma
by an act of self-immolation, which there was
no justification for demanding of him.
# # #
The awkwardness of the situation tints
created is only equalled by the stupidity w hich
rendered it necessary. This stupidity, more-
over, is not chargeable on Gladstone. No one
in England or elsewhere will dream of ascribing
so obvious a blunder to that exceedingly astute
politician. And, moreover, the grave and
abiding consequences which have become inevit-
able, are not so inconvenient to Gladstone as
to the Throne. The stupidity proceeds from the
same quarter as the influence which was instru
mental in having Labouchere kept out of th'
Cabinet. The actual result has been to exhib t
the Sovereign in the character of an individual
giving effect to a personal animosity and spirit
of revenge. The efforts which have been made
to obscure the fact that the reason for Labor
ciiere’s exclusion is his personal antagonism to
the continual demands of the Crown for grants
to members of the Queen’s family, have been
faint and ineffectual. The truth glares out
with dazzling radiance, and penetrates the
paltry veils and curtains of shuffling evasion
and prevarication with which it has been
attempted to extinguish its illumination, ft
has been in vain that Gladstone has, tardily
been prompted to divert attention from the
essential circumstances. Gladstone has not
even ventured to deny that it was in deference
to the ascertained will of the Sovereign that he
passed over Labouchere. All that he ventures
to advance is that he assumes the responsibility
for the fact that Labouchere was not invited
to become a member of the Ministry, and
that Labouchere’s name was not submitted to
Queen Victoria. The first admission admits
nothing. Gladstone’s responsibility existed
whether his act was spontaneous or vicarious
As for the latter statement, it is ridiculous and
alters the facts not one tittle. The question
in the public mind—or rather the impression to
be removed, for there is no doubt in the public
mind—is that the Queen had caused Gladstone
to be made aware that she wished that Mr.
Labouchere’s name should not be submitted to
her.
* * *
The immediate outcome of this is precisely
the contrary to what plainly was intended.
The design was, obviously, to diminish Labou-
chere’s importance as a public man and a
politician. The thing achieved has been to
even magnify his influence. Included in the
Cabinet and surrounded by the heavy Whigs
who constitute it, Labouchere must have been
almost effaced. He would have found himself
controlled by his colleagues. His vivid per-
sonality would have been repressed by the mere
weight of the heavy - witted mediocrities
associated with him. Even his pen, from whose
point electric sparkles of penetrating satire
have so regularly burnt holes in the privileges
of “ the classes,” would have been deprived of
some of its destructive brilliance. The Right
Hon. Henry Labouchere would have been a
gigantic shadow compared with the compact
efficient substance of Henry Labouchere, 1 the
Radical freelance. To lose such an opportunity
of smothering a character so formidable would,
by itself, have been a blunder indicative of the
most abject stupidity. But when the commis-
sion of that display of folly involved the
augmentation equally of Labouchere’s inclina-
tion towards destructive criticism and of the
potency of his exertions, it required an inspira-
tion of really fatuous imbecility to enter upon
a course so suicidal. The condensed purport of
the Royal interference in the composition of
the Cabinet is, whether intentionally or not,
made too pointedly plain for any rational being
to misapprehend it. The public man who in
the exercise of his functions as a representative
of the Commons of Britain resists the deter-
mination of the Sovereign to exploit the
Treasury for the enrichment—at the expense of
the taxpayer—of the Royal brood and such
princes and princelets, wealthy or pauper, as
wed among them, is officially warned by this
incident that he will incur the personal
animosity of the occupant of the Throne, that
such animosity will find vent in vindictiveness,
and, what is more important, that the Sovereign
will refuse to accept as an Executive Minister
persons who dare to commit themselves to such
resistance to the Royal will and the Royal '
greed.
* * *
There has been no such situation in England
since the reign of James 11. That is to say,
not that James sought to provide for his
children, but that he personally resented oppo-
sition to his will. The cases of Fox and of
Canning are scarcely parallel. Neither of these
statesmen represented a growing political
theory. And, above all, neither of them, not-
withstanding Canning’s literary brilliancy, lived
in the days of widely-circulated periodicals.
Labouchere (a man, by the way, of aristocratic
birth) is a far more formidable personage than
either of these. He has wealth sufficient to make
the emolument of office a matter of indifference
to him, and he controls an organ which has had
more influence in moulding public opinion than
the Times and all the other so-called great
dailies combined. This influence is not due to
any supremacy of Labouchere’s journalistic
talent over the combined accomplishments ol
the galaxy of able writers who provide the
articles which are published in the daily press.
It may be attributed to the circumstance that
whereas, depressed by the influence of bour-
geois proprietors, the writers on the daily
papers have to be content with skimming the
surface of things, and dealing with every matter
in that spiritof conventionality which reduces the
most brilliant to dullness, Labouchere’s paper
treats, without hesitation, of the thoughts and
tendencies which are as yet but embryotic in
the public mind, and which have not yet
received the sanctification of universal accept-
ance. Thus, while the daily journals and
others which follow their example continually
utter homilies upon old and threadbare fabrics
of thought, and are consequently supremely
uninteresting, Labouchere’s Truth persists
in exploring new regions and generally m
a fresh and dashing style, and is, therefore,
read with avidity. "No one observes with more
than languid interest the tillage of an oft-culti-
vated field. But the exploration of new regions,
or the development of original inventions,
irresistibly attracts eager and curious attention.
« • *
The ideas for the pronouncement of which
Labouchere has been subjected to affront are
naturally enough shared by millions of Britons.
That the Sovereign should be liberally 7
is generally assented to ; but that the taxpayer
should be levied upon to maintain in affluence
everyone of her immediate descendants is wide y
resented. Moreover, the related ideas wluc
prevail in the minds of people who think thus,
and of which Labouchere has been
guished expounder, are also widely dittusec r


I t ]ji a wa y that gentleman is the representative
of a powerful and rapidly increasing political
rty. Hence, in declaring war against Labou-
ciiehe the Sovereign has, in effect, set herself in
direct personal antagonism to a numerous sec-
tion of her subjects—a section, moreover, which
must according to all human probabilities and
to the precedents taught by history, before long
assume a distinct and predominant place in the
Commons. Nothing can be more apparent
than the tendency of"the venerable distinctions
of Whig and Tory, Conservative and Liberal, to
crumble. These have no real basis left in them.
The political interests which they respectively
pretend to represent have become obliterated.
The real hue of antagonisms has shifted, and it
seems inevitable that before many years shall
Pave elapsed the absence of genuine differences
between a Conservative earl and a Liberal duke
will have made itself telt, while the real and
intense incompatibility of the aims of the
“masses” and the “classes” will compel a
corresponding re-constrnction of parties in the
Parliament of the country. In that event it
will be the Labouchere stamp of men who will
be leaders of the popular cause, and the episode
under notice has made the Sovereign the leader
on the other side. In such case, a Parliamen-
tary victory would become equivalent to a
defeat for the monarch, and the inevitable cry
of vee victis ! will be fraught with a signifi-
cance menacing to the very institution of
Royalty. A more egregious blunder than
has just been committed has seldom been
recorded in the history of England, and never
without being followed by momentous conse-
quences. It is perhaps natural that a Queen
should, owing to the atmosphere of adulation
and trumpery precedence in which she lives,
miscalculate altogether the potency of an ex-
clusion from the great offices of State. It is
conceivable that it might appear to her that
her frown can blast and degrade. But the day
when such blighting results were accomplish-
able by royal displeasure have gone by. Labou-
chere, the Radical of “noble” birth, might
have been nearly annihilated by conferring office
on him. By refusing it to him on the ground
that he is a Radical, he has been rendered
terrible. He has been aggrandized to the point
of disputing with the Sovereign the affection
and confidence of the people of England. It is
cabled that at Northampton a meeting ended in
a general melee, cheers being given for the
Queen and countercheers for Labouchere —her
political rival.
The Keely Humbug.
A VOLUME dealing with the alleged bichloride of
gold cure of dipsomania, and entitled, “ Is it a
xModern Miracle ? ” lias been sent to us for
review. It is undoubtedly a modern miracle
that although Maudsley has lived and written,
that although every scientist of note who has
devoted any attention to the subject has re-
garded dipsomania as the result of an organic
lesion, there are still people in the world pre-
pared to believe in the Keely remedy. He
who pines for either alcohol or opium is yearn-
ing for the balance of the wholesome constitu-
tion which Nature has denied him * his thirst is
a protest only against mal formation. Some-
where at the back of the old truism that “a
man cannot be too careful in the selection of
his ancestors ” lies the secret of all inebriety ;
and we who have grasped this fact can have but
little faith in the efficacy of bichloride of gold,
or any other nostrum.
* 9 #
The question of the drunkard’s cure is not one
of reformation but of re-formation ; and to dig
about and manure a weeping willow in the hope
of converting it into an oak-tree would be no
more absurd than the injection of an inert salt
of gold with the hope of changing organic
tendencies. We have dived into Mr. Calhoun’s
voluminous record of candid personalities,
pseudo-scientific theories, and doubtful testi-
monials, and have emerged from it with the
firm conviction that, as a modern miracle,
the actual giving of gold cannot compare
in its results with those of an allied treat-
ment now adopted by a leading physician of
our acquaintance, who first catches his dipso-
maniac, and having filled his hypodermic
syringe with pure distilled water, draws parti-
cular attention to the golden needle. Then
he solemnly makes the injection, assuring the
patient, at the same time, that no craving can
possibly return for ten hours. Exactly at the
end of the period named, the thirst becomes
intolerable, and the victim comes back for a repe-
tition of his specific—the intervals lengthening,
until one dose a week is found to be sufficient,
and the patient goes away with the bright,
thankful face of a poisoned man who has found
his antidote, one in whom persuasion and
belief had ripened into faith, and faith become
a passionate intuition.
# # «
The whole of the world’s drinkers belong to
the comprehensive natural order known among
scientists as epileptics, an order characterised
by an incontinence of nerve - element that,
lengthened out, spells “ tragedy,” and extend-
ing from Swedenborg who made a religion to
Mary Jones, who last night made a beast of
herself. It requires but little insight to under-
stand the slight basis upon which the cures
recorded by Mr. Calhoun in his volume are
founded. A pathetic desire to be cured and a
tendency to believe in quackery are permanent
characteristics of all drunkards, and the patent-
medicine-taker himself supplies the active
principle in the faith and hope engendered
• # #
Extensive research into the physiological
action of gold has shown that it does not belong
to that division of metals known and valued
as nerve-stimulants, and it is difficult to under-
stand why either zinc or arsenic, with their
established and well-founded reputations, was
not chosen as the basis of this physic. It may
be that the glamour which hangs about the
name of Gold—and this nowhere more than in
ankeeland—has been of commercial value to
ie cute promoters, and we are the more in-
towards this belief by seeing that a
elbourne firm has gone “one better” and
ciscovored a “ diamond remedy” for inebriety,
only remains now for some enterprising Aus-
tralian to dissolve pearls, or make a syrup of
sapphires.
9 * m
And just as we have done writing, an extract
from London Referee is handed us :
Like the Medical Society of London, I have not much
faith in the so-called Gold Cure for drunkenness, which
has been brought over here by its American inventor.
The objection of the Medical Society to Mr. Keely’s nos-
trum is that its composition is not disclosed, and that it
is being run, consequently, on the lines of the ordinary
quack remedy. If the beneficial results of the “cure”
were undeniable, that objection would have little weight
with me. I should accept it on its merits, and willingly
allow its shrewd Yankee patentee a pecuniary reward for
his cleverness. But I am not at all convinced that
inveterate drunkenness can. be cured, or that it is even
worth curing.- Lately I have happened to be able to
devote a good deal of attention to this sinister subject,
and the results are curious, not to say unexpected. The
medical books will tell you that about halt the number
of habitual drunkards suffer from the hereditary
taint. This, I am convinced, is not an accurate
statement of the facts. The percentage is much higher,
so much so that I am in some doubt as to whether
habitual drunkenness can exist at all without hereditary
predisposition. In two of the worst examples that I have
been examining there is no hereditary taint apparent to
the uneducated eye, neither the parents nor the grand-
parents, nor any of the near collaterals of the victims,
having been addicted to the vice. And yet nerves are at
the bottom of the business.
*99
“ Nerves” conduce to drunkenness in two ways. The
well-known “ sinking ” feeling, which many women allege
as their reason for taking a drop of something, is supposed
to be an “effect” of drinking. My own observation
leads me to regard it often as a genuine “ cause,” having
its origin in a congenital weakness of the nervous system.
Another class of habitual drunkards require “ soothing”
rather than stimulating, and it will be lound in these
cases that the victims, especially women, before taking
to the brandy-bottle, have had recourse to opiates
of various kinds. Doubtless indulgence in drink aggra-
vates whatever mischief may already exist, but the
nervous predisposition may be such as to render life
a burden without it and to unfit its victims for the
ordinary responsibilities of citizenship. By keeping these
wretched people from stimulants you do not cure them,
you merely drive the evil into other channels, one of
which may be insanity if the excitability of the victim is
very marked. Now, these various nervous conditions are
as much hereditary' as any specific complaint could be.
It is a mistake to suppose that predisposition does not
exist because the father or mother of a drunkard has not
been addicted to drunkenness. What is inherited is not
a taste for drink, as many worthy people suppose, but a
nervous instability, which drinking relieves, for the time
being, at least.
9 9 9
In order to cope with such physical predisposition as I
have described, the gold cure, or any other cure, would
have to effect a radical change in the nervous sys-
tem, and, if this could be done, we might hope to
eradicate not merely drunkenness, but a score of
other ailments equally fatal to their victims. Malfor-
mations, for example, are due to some defect in the
nutritive processes, which have their seat in the nervous
system. Will any American professor have the hardi-
hood to come forward and undertake to cure the halt
and the hump-backed? I opine not, because there the
uselessness of his nostrum would be too apparent. All
these mysteries of growth and predisposition are really
too deep-seated for our poor human science to compass.
We can only verify the facts and deplore them. As to
the part which predisposition plays in drinking, it is
hardly possible to express a definite opinion. Not a few
people, fortunately for them, are just as incapable of
taking to drink, no matter what their temptations may
be, as others are prone to it. To me alcohol is really dis-
tasteful, although, in the interests of good-fellowship, I
have given it many trials. I ought to have been a rabid
teetotal lecturer, “ instead of w'hich ” as the good old
judge put it, Nature has cursed me with an inquiring
mind, a predisposition to which I am as much a victim as
the habitual drunkard is to his.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“A Gaol Chaplain” on Flogging.
A person subscribing himself “A Gaol Chap-
lain” writes to the S.M. Herald recommend-
ing copious and enthusiastic floggings as a
remedy for larrikinism. Also, he wants the
floggings administered five minutes or so after
conviction, so that if the law has got hold of
the wrong larrikin there may be no time to dis-
cover the mistake ; and he doesn’t want the
offender sent to prison—where “ A Gaol Chap-
lain ” can minister to his soul—at all, from
which it would seem that he finds himself
unequal to saving brands from the burning,
and thinks it would be a lot easier simply to
flog the brand, and turn him loose in all his
original wickedness. There is food for much
reflection in all this. God put skin on man, and
His humble follower, the “Gaol Chaplain,’’wants
to take it off. Being a chaplain he necessarily
regards the matter from a spiritual standpoint,
and his utterance evidently means that he finds
preaching ineffectual as a means of saving
souls, and wants to try flaying instead. If this
device is effectual as a means of redemption,
then The Bulletin is of opinion that it ought
to be tried all round ; it is certainly better
that a fashionable congregation, for instance,
should be whaled in this world than that it
should be lost in the next, and as preaching
seems woefully ineffectual, it might possibly be
a good idea to omit the customary sermon, and
simply flog the congregation twice on Sun-
day, and then take up a collection. This
theory, of course, is based on the natural
assumption that “ A Gaol Chaplain ” re-
gards the sudden removal of the outer
cuticle as a means of grace —in which case, by
the way, he ought to do the flogging himself,
the means of grace being distinctly his special
department. And, if he doesn’t so regard it,
and merely recommends it as a substitute for
confinement under the blessed influence of his
ministrations in order to save trouble, then, in
that case also, he ought to do it himself. He
is paid by the State to show every prisoner the
evil of his ways, and if 30 or 40 per cent, of his
work is taken off his shoulders by his proposed
system, then it seems only reasonable that lie
should fill in his time with flogging. More-
over, if he wasn’t willing to do the flogging
himself, he shouldn’t recommend it ; and
if he is too fat and flabby and short-winded
to make skin and blood fly in the orthodox
Botany Bay manner, he should stand aside
and make room for a younger and more
muscular apostle. Further, seeing that
the chaplain in question evidently regards
the cat as a good and honourable thing. The
Bulletin would like to know if he habitually
shakes hands with the official who uses that
implement when he meets him, and treats him
as a man and a brother, for, if not, then he
doesn’t live up to his professions. If a man
demands that a certain thing be done, and
won’t do it himself, and looks down on the
person who does it, then he is several varieties
of a skunk. Lastly, this paper would like to
know that chaplain’s conscientious opinion
about the hangman and the fiogger’s prospects
in the great hereafter ; and it asks him if, when
he buries the deceased cat-artist, and alludes to
him as a brother who is planted in the sure and
certain hope of a blessed resurrection, he really
and truly hopes to sit alongside of and chum
up with a glorified flogger in the fields of
Asphodel ? Amen.

Labour-Force a Commodity.
Dear Bulletin, —Bishop Ooe was perfectly, if
cold-bloodedly, correct when he stated at the
annual meeting of the Charity Organisation
Society that labour was only a commodity ;
under existing conditions it is nought else. His
deduction, however, was as perfectly illogical:
it is just because labour is a commodity, if on
no higher grounds, that the seller is justified in
selling it at the best possible price. As Las-
salle put the matter when describing the
“ Iron Law of Wages ” to the Frankfort
labourers :
You believe, perhaps, fellow-citizens, that you are
human beings—that you are men. Speaking from the
standpoint of political economy, you make a terrible
mistake. Speaking from the standpoint of political
economy, you are nothing but a commodity, a high price
tor which increases your numbers, just as a high price for
stockings increases the number of stockings, if there are
not enough of them ; while you are swept away, your
number is diminished by smaller wages, just as if you were
vermin against which society wages war.
If the labourers of this country be business men,
they will not imagine that the monopolists look
at the wages question from a sentimental point
of view, and, looking at it from a practical point
of view themselves, will take the advice of Karl
Marx in his workman’s appeal for a normal
working-day and be “ saving,” as they are often
advised to lie, by the godly and stingy. Saving,
yes ; but not of their money—of their bodily
powers. They will see that the employer does
not exhaust a vitality which should represent
thirty years endurance inside ten years at ten
years' purchase, thus thieving two-thirds of
their labour-force and making no monetary
return.—-Yours, George Black.

The Wages of Slaver.
.The sort of encouragement which literary
eminence meets with from Governments in
Great Britain has been brought into high relief
by the distribution of so-called honours by the
out-going Premier. Not a single writer has
shared in the shower of “ honorary ” distinc-
tion. It seems not to have occurred to Salis-
bury to distinguish for the admiration of the
nation a single individual among the men whose
talents as journalists have been devoted towards
emphasising the merits and extenuating the
defects of his Administration. As for the
litterateurs, as distinguished from the jour-
nalists, these have been similarly neglected.
All his consideration has been absorbed by the
fat proprietors of the daily journals, persons
among whom not an individual selected by him
for titular distinction lias ever evinced the
faintest indication of literary gifts. It is coolly
assumed that the bestowal of titles upon men
who wade in advertisements and grow rich by
thattraffic and by the profits derived from sweat-
ing writers and compositors, is equivalent to a
“ recognition of literature.” The tuft-hnnting
Lawson alias Levy, the Jew who is ashamed of
his race, is a type of all the rest—the Jaffrays,
the Captain Armstrongs— suggestive name !
the Borthwicks, are all proprietors of news-
papers, and not one has ever written anything
notable. Even Queen Victoria has done better
than that in conferring a peerage on the
courtier-laureate Tennyson, and a knighthood
on Arthur lady-HELPS who patched up the
grammar and spelling of her own “reminis-
cences.” The dignity business is running to
seed altogether. The ideal is to elevate for
admiration the noblest of their generation, the
real is to barter titles for a consideration. The
peerages are mostly sold to enterprising specu-
lators who make heavy investments in the
electioneering funds of their party, and
the small-goods are awarded to sycophants,
turn-coats, and other vermin. Sometimes it is
a nigger, some Jamsetjee Jejeebhoy, or a
Chow like Whampoa, of the Straits Settle-
ments, and sometimes a bogus Republican like
Dibbs who swaps his loudly-proclaimed Repub-
licanism for a Jamsetjee-ship. There seems to
be a merciful Providence watching over Demo-
cratic aspirations. Were a sovereign once to
catch hold of the idea of confining the bestowal
of titles to persons really distinguished by their
abilities or their merit, the Democratic and
Republican propaganda would receive a fearful
set-back. But, thanks to an overruling Provi-
dence, some lying spirit is let loose to bam-
boozle monarchs and Ministers, and to put
it into their heads to pick john davies and
r. b. smith and the like for special honour. And
the latest welcome move in this direction is,
according to recent indications, that Her
Majesty will be advised that there is no man
in Australia so fit to be grappled to her
soul as that noble and superbly gifted being,
Dr. renwick.
Some more “Thin Edge of the Wedge.”
Some time ago a measure was passed in New
South Wales, providing for the adoption—to a
very small and limited extent —of the “ better-
ment ” principle in the construction of public
v/orks. It was passed because the Legislature
decided that the principle was just and neces-
sary, and it was restricted in obedience to the
old cant wheeze which asserts that it is sound
statesmanship always to start with the thin
end of the wedge, and not remove the whole of
an old, condemned tumor at once, lest the nation
should wake up in the night and cry for its
lost disease. Minister Lyne, it is now an-
nounced, has a bill on hand providing for
a further extension of this principle, and
The Bulletin trusts that on this occasion
the New South Wales Government will adopt
the whole principle in one act—one-sixth of a
principle is of no special value to anybody.
The one thing that the people of Australia
should never forget is that, for want of this
system in the past, something like £100,000,000
has been utterly fooled away. The theory on
which it is based is that the capitalist whose
property is enhanced in value by Government
works should pay to the State a proportion of
the profit which the State thus creates for him.
As the additional value is created entirely by
the nation it is justly entitled to take it all,
bub if it merely annexes 75 per cent, of tha
amount, and m'akes the ungrateful landlord a
free gift of the balance, it is possible to raisv
enough to build almost all the public works of
the future without floating another loan
from now to the end of futurity. The
system of borrowing a million to construct a
railway which thereby adds, probably, a million
and a half to the value of adjacent lands for the
benefit of some gilded absentee is too insane to
require comment. What the country demands
is that the betterment principle shall be
adopted whole—that when any Government or
municipal work enhances the value of private
property, whether to the extent of £5 or
£5,000,000, the State shall annex a certain
fixed proportion, and a large one, of that
increased value for public purposes. If the
amount thus taxed exceeds the amount spent
upon the wont, so much the better for the
State. It is conceivable, under some circum-
stances, that an undertaking which cost only
£500,000 might add £1.000,000 to adjacent
values, and in such a case, on the 75 per cent,
basis, the nation would possess an asset worth
half-a-million which cost it nothing, it would be
£250,000 in pocket—and the adjoining land-
lords would have received £250,000 for nothing,
which is much more than sufficient for any
body of landlords.
Old Billy Brooks, of Brisbane, now an
Honorable, and ascended to the Upper House,
where so many good ironmongers go, has of late
become a bit of a Tory, but he used to be in the
front of the battle against the Kanaka slave-
trade what time Sam. Griffith was the David
of the anti-Kanak host; and his gorge has
risen nearly through the top of his head at the
pro-nigger-stealing dispatch of Governor Nor-
man. Writes the Governor :
The opposition to the reintroduction of black-labour in
the colony has not been and has been mainly con-
fined to general denunciations by ministers of religion or
others, and to the efforts made to prevent any action
which may directly or indirectly be supposed to tend to
lower the high rates of wages which now obtain in
Queensland.
Billy writes to Brisbane Courier denouncing
this as “a disrespectful and incautious sen-
tence,I’and 1 ’ and says the “ opinions are so wide of
universally known facts as to be positively mis-
leading ” which appears to be a rather round-
about periphrasis for “ confounded lies.” Wil-
liam also remarks, with respect to the Gover-
nor’s sentence that “ I am lead to believe that
there would be much opposition at the present
time to the importation of Asiatics into the
colony,” that—
When his Excellency lias to be led up to a belief that
the sun shines every day at noon, it sets one asking, is
there no friend who can venture to suggest to his Excel-
lency that he write no more despatches about black-labour
until the general election is Over?
And appears to think that while each holding
one of his Excellency’s ears open, Griffith and
MTlwraith manage to have a hand to spare
for his vice-regal nose.

Editor Fitchett, in the alleged Australian
Review of Reviews, remarks that the ridicule
heaped on Sir G. Republican Dibbs for accept-
ing a title is “not at all sensible,” and proceeds
to illustrate his idea that titles are “ not incom-
patible with even the drab simplicity of a repub-
lic ” by asserting that be counted 2(X) decorous
persons sitting in a Parisian park one sunny
afternoon, and that there are more “ Colonels,”
“ Judges,” and “ Senators ” to the square mile
in the United States than in any other country
on earth. It does not suit the acute-minded
Mr. Fitchett to perceive the difference
between the action of the avowed Repub-
lican accepting a tifcie ‘at the hands of a
Monarchy—and that the very Monarchy against
which his sentiments were addressed—and the
American colonel, whose title is often a
sobriquet, or the decorated Frenchman, whose
badge of honour is the symbol of the Republic’s
appreciation of some service done for the
Republic. “A sensible man accepts the usages
of the social system to which he belongs,” says
the Fitchett, in defending the apostacy of the
Dibbs. Beautiful laisser-faire ! If “ sensible ”
were synonymous with fatness, comfort,
bourgeois respectability, and blind, callous
indifference to the feelings and fortunes of
neighbours and brothers, there would be a little
reason and lots of truth in the pompous
generalisation of our reverend coutemporarv.

Says Melbourne Am;us :
The State is in admittedly an awkward position as rail-
way owner, since it must face competition in some
quarters. It should he entitled to make every reasonable
effort to secure business, hut when it goes beyond reason
it does serious injustice. In the first place, the com-
petitor suffers a twofold wrong. He is driven off the road
or water as the case may he, thereby losing trade; and
he also, as a unit in the community, has to pay his share
of the railway deficit caused by the cutting down of rates.
From these statements the Conservative hurdy-
gurdy deduces the conclusion that the State has
no right to compete with private carriers
because that involves “suppression of private
enterprise by Government.” We look at it the
other way : the private individual has no right
to compete with Governments in the mighty
enterprises which some Stales think right to
handle for fear of gigantic monopolies laying
hold of them. If rates are cut down it should
be for the general good ; cut down they should
bo to the very lowest limit that will pay
interest on expenditure and replace worn-out
capital. The State is no prolit-mongering
machine.
Dear Bulletin, —Six years ago I was pot-
sticking at a Balmoral River (Q.) wool scour, and
heard the squatter congratulate himself on the
success of theWolseley sheep-shearing machines,
then on trial, which would, he said, make shearing
of the future contract-work, with probably Chinese
at weekly wages. This has not been verified, but,
judging from the utterances of a prominent sheep-
breeder at the recent pastoralists’ meeting in
Sydney, a move may shortly be made. The
speaker, referring to present rates, said the price
for machine-shearing should be fixed on a different
basis, as the extras in supplying the motive-power,
&c., brought the cost up to 255. to 275. (id. per 100.
Which means that machine-shearers will be asked
to concede the extras. As the inventors claim a
gain of several ozs. wool for every sheep shorn by
their machines as compared with hand-shearing,
one would think the wool-kings might thus balance
their ledgers. But no mention was made of heavier
clips at the meeting, although it was emphasised
that the machines lightened the toil of the shearer.
—J.J.C.

810 Macquarie-slreet North, Sydney, August 31,189?.
My Dkar Moorabinua,—
Lady Charles Scott, in a pearl-grey silk, completely
covered with black lace and topped oft by a black coronet
toque with a spray of myrtle, opened N.S.W. Horticul-
tural Society’s daffodil and camellia show, held in Sydney
Y.M.C.A. hall, the other Friday. “Rule Britannia” is
the air that warns you the Admiraless is coming, so, of
course, the proceedings opened with it, and everybody
remarked that Lady S. walked up the aisle unassisted
and not on the arm of a committee-man, which is the
“properest thing to do.”
I hope the Horticultural Society won’t encore the
experiment of having a floral contest in the tiny Y.M.C.A.
The crowd (a small one on half-crown day, owing to
uncertain weather) positively jostled one another, and I
am sure the exhibits suffered from people catching in the
top branches as they passed. The pieces de resistance-
daffodils —were very fine, and they had romantic names,
too—the only clumsy cognomen I saw was “ William
Wilks.” But, after all, naming different species of the
same flower is very like christening each grape in a
cluster. One petal tilts to the left instead of the right,
and the Susannah Jane becomes the Ethel Maude—but
only a perfect dab at green-stuff could tell them apart.
I stood beside a fierce, military-looking old gentleman,
and he read the names, “Grande Monarque” and “ La
reine Jaune,” on what looked like the same flower.
“ Well, I’ll be shot,” quoth he, “ if I can see any differ-
ence !” And I couldn’t, either.
The flower-show revealed nothing gaspingly lovely in
the clothes line. Sassiety is doing the usual chrysalis act
before the Spring dress parade. One long black sacque
coat with Watteau back and collar hanging out like the
lip of a pint-pot was nobbily worn by a slim blonde with a
decided “jag on,” as Billy Emerson says. Somebody
sported an Eton jacket in dark-blue serge. Also Sjdney’s
lady medico was there in a serge coat and a pink dickey,
and her hands in her pockets—as usual.
By the way, there were one or two displays of bush
flowers at the show, and I was secretly much amused at
the spectacle of several women taking eager notes of the
Latin names of the fuzzy, scrubby anscmic-looking shrubs
which garland Australia. They had an eye to coming out
strong at ensuing summer picnics, I suppose. “ Will you
be good enough to pluck mo a bloom of that beautiful
care canem pro bono publico, Mr. Jones?” she will say
with an airy wave towards a common bottle-brush.
Emerson’s minstrels, at the Garrick, attracted an
audience the night I smiled upon the entertainment,
which reminded one of the crowds that used to back up
J.L.T.—old stagers, you know, have a perfect mania
for being wafted back a hundred years more or less. There
are one or two silvery tenors in the troupe, and, if they
would only pipe something more reasonable than curdled
sentiments about meeting Nell in a dell, how you never
can tell how you love her so well, the dress circle would
be truly grateful. One thing I liked about Emerson’s
minstrels—none of the jokes are broad enough to send
even the most modest maiden behind her fan, so papa
can take the girls without a moment’s hesitation.
People who stopped at the same Hobart hash-house as
“ Lord and Lady Leamington,” say that the airs and
graces of these amusing humbugs were quite up to
peerage-pitch. One day “ her ladyship ” was idly strum-
ming on the drawing-rocm piano (possibly, “ Sparkling
wine! gift of the gods above, boys!” which she used to
sing in such pretty tights in the ’mime), when a vehicle
drove up to the door. “Is that our carriage, deah?”
asked she of Lord L., who was looking out of the window.
“ Some people might call it a cawwidge, pet,” he replied,
“ but it’s not one you would dwive in !”
Talking of airs and graces, reminds me that the anti-
Australian Anglo-Indian still infests the high sea between
London and Bombay, and shows no signs of abating half-
an-inch of his curry-coloured sneer when anything which
has cut its teeth on a corn-cob is seen following its
luggage up the gangway. Quite recently, on a mail*
steamer coming this way, two rather puckered and
liverish-looking Anglo-Indiennes were observed at table
on the second day out Jo shiver as if stricken with
paralysis, then glare in a maniacal manner at their
fellow - passengers, as if seeking for some visage as
bilious as their own. And, on finding it not, they rose
precipitately and fled, making uncharitable remarks on
the utter impossibility of dining with Australians as the
went 1
Not that I am hotly indignant against these ladies—
for, bless you, it all depends on what “ representative ”
was stoking provender into himself with both hands on
the blade of his pen-knife at the moment when their
feelings overcame them. I have sat up aloft in
galleries and watched my countrymen experiencing the
pleasures of the table, and I’ll make a solemn affidavit (if
anybody will show me how) that at an altitude of forty
feet I have distinctly heard the soup-suction.
The funniest thing on ship-board—on the big, gilded,
be-caipeted mail-boats, I mean—must be the crony
dinners that spring up every now and again. Elaborate
invitations go forth and are elaborately accepted, and the
passengers “ not in it ” feel very much out of it for days
previously. But when the evening comes the guests form
a party at a separate table in the saloon, and feed from
the same menu as the rest of the passengers. It seem-
ingly doesn’t take much to amuse people on the boring
briny.
Harping on maritime subjects—l wonder if it ever
occurs to the aspirant for the fatal position of a brass-
buttoned anything on a mail-boat what a Father Know-
All he has got to be? Besides boxing the compass and
lying the wheel he must know how to be a fascinating
llirl—a bon-t n ant—and a variety actor. Especially the
last, if he wants to run an amusing ship. In every fin-
officer’s kit there are two or three complete
theatrical costumes, and when the fun shows signs of
flagging he dons the torn coat of “ Killaloe” or the smut-
covered apparel of the “Bogie Man,” and does his turn
to keep the ship awake. If industry and strict attention
to all sorts of business merit reward the tars that o’er
the ocean sail well deserve all the hugs they’re supposed
to get.
What a rooted habit the average Britisher has of
alluding to everything as “my,” even though his sole
worldly asset is a paper - collar ! One noble lord’s
relation in Australia is quite noted for his extreme
progressiveness, although most of the things he deals
with belong to somebody else. The other day he went
forth on a borrowed animal with a loaned gun to shoot
something—but this is how he put it: “I was making
towards my home on my mare when I saw an eagle-hawk.
I galloped my mare home—got my gun—but found the
hawk had flown.” It was the same individual who once
roused a country house in the small hours, and when
everybody pelted to the spot coolly told the foremost
person “to be sure—in the morning—to serve my potatoes
in their jackets ! ”
This month the Spring gaieties commence. The A. J.C.
meet begins on 10th inst. Two Governors, Hopetoun and
Norman, with their wives and suites, are to stay with
Lord and Lady Jersey during the races. There will be
balls on the 14th, loth, and 16th insts., Mayor and
Mayoress Manning leading off in the Town Hall, Govern-
ment House second, the Union Club third. The Medical
Congress will also enliven matters, and I feel glad to hear
that the deeply scientific and improving Royal Society of
N.S.W. intend entertaining the itinerant medicine-men at
a conversazione in the great hall of the University. That
is the whole extent of the Spring whirligig, I believe, with
the addition of big dances and little dinners and all that
sort of thing.
N.S.W. Art Society’s annual exhibition will be formally
opened by Lord Jersey on sth inst., but the fun really
begins at the private view on Saturday, 3rd inst. The
show will be a regular bumper of art this Spring. I hear
that the hanging committee have worn themselves out
trying to give everybody a good peg, and with 305 ex
hi bits (a baker’s dozen of which are works of sculpture)
and the very meagre wall-space at their disposal, one can
easily imagine how exciting their task has been.
The “management” of a desperately insolvent Queens-
land church hit on an ingenious plan to galvanize the
bank-account, so I’m told. They begged a huge cake
from a member of the congregation, and induced five bar*
maids (one from each local shanty) to be candidates for
it—the result to be decided by ballot—each voter paying
a shilling for the privilege of putting Tottie at the top
of the poll. The ladies canvassed so alluringly that the
original issue of 150 tickets was raised to 1000, and in the
end the church made £5O out of a mere hunk of dough
and plums, with a 999-to-l chance you don’t get it. The
hook-nosed sweep-promoter had better look to it, or the
church will cut him out in the running.
Churches have the coolest way of grabbing that the
world has ever sum. Somebody’s servant came from the
family’s place of worship, the other Sunday, and solemnly
infoimed her master that he bad been “assessed” by his
spiritual guardians at £5O, and that she, Mary, had been
assessed at £5. And both had to pay up merrily, too—or
else be cast into the outer darkness.

The “usher of the black rod” (who might he
arrested for having no visible means of support
when he gets those historic hosen on) presented the
