1 want to suggest a change in opera-staging. There is
one auburn-haired, pretty-I’olly visaged chorister, who is
always first to lead on the frantic mob, or the happy
peasants, or the blythe and merry flower-sellers. She is

everybody is getting tired of her. A mob that is always
headed by one carrot-topped individual is handicapped by
always seeming to be the same mob, no matter what sort
of mob it’s s'U'poscd to be.

John Struck Oil Williamson saw fit to
resurrect the merry “Pepita” at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney) on Saturday, and, judging by the howls
of a crowded gallery, the suppressed chuckles of
a pressed-down and running-over dress-circle and
the shrieks of the surging stalls, the operatic
corpse was galvanised at the right moment. Yet
Flora Graupner, as Pepita (too solemnly in earnest
everywhere to give a playful rendering of a charac-
ter that easily degenerates into shrewishness),
forced her warble right through, with the inevit-
able result that she was sometimes fully half a tone
out. Her castanet solo was very much so-so, and
the stirring toreador song sounded as if she was
merely asking somebody to pass the butter,
please. Leumane’s Inigo was the same Inigo
to a dot —a little fatter as becomes the
placid agriculturist—or perhaps it was corn-
cobs in the gullet that made his infrequent vocal
opportunities sound as if he was choking. As
Pataques, Lauri scored heavily in the duet in act
two, though he didn’t look such a cocky little
warrior as Elton used to—but, then, the gigantic
Marnock, with his aggravating tremolo, was the
“ Bombardos ” of other days—and Ryley—lovely
youth ! —though he goes up high enough, didn’t
fit Bombardos’s clothes horizontally. Violet
Varley and Howard Vernon (Inez and Pedrillo)
deserve a lot of nice remarks which they are not
going to get, and the rest of the billed people may
continue to think that the interest centred round
them individually for all The Bulletin cares.
Fireworks in a coal-cellar only feebly expresses
the radianoe of the raise cn scene.

A noticeable feature about the “Pepita”
revival was the way in which Flora Graupner
(who seemed tired out) plucked up heart in tights
and lost it again in petticoats—though it’s gene-
rally the other way round. In the fetching cadet
uniform (which also set off Varley’s rounded form
like a little model of a doosid fine woman, you
know) and the gorgeous black-and-gold bull-
fighter’s rig, the fair Flora got quite perky. But
a return to petticoats sobered her instantly, and
the queen scene (how all of ’em do love to stalk
round with ten yards of stuff hanging off behind,
and a tin crown on) could have occurred in any
well-regulated tragedy. By the way, Flora ought
to learn the use and abuse of the sword-scabbard.
Half the time when she sported a skewer she
dropped the sheath, and the devoted chorus had

that it mightn’t rise up and smite her in her reck-

What is the matter with the frantic ad-
mirers of the warbling divinities where have all
the bouquets got to? Night in, night out, the
leading ladies trill and ogle and point their toes
at the orchestra, but never a daisy whistles over

aren’t insolvent is a puzzle. And even the old

The “New Magdalen” was revived at
Sydney Opera House, on Saturday night, in the
presence of a rather enthusiastic audience. There
is a certain amount of realism in Wilkie Collins’
drama, but not much, and it is always the unreal
parts that the audience principally whoops f o i
The realism consists of the lost Magdalen herself
—or it would do so if she continued lost— th
woman who is talked to death by respectable hatra*
furies, and spinsters, and assaulted with the birr’
hard-bound Bible of the brassy preacher and
walked upon by the wild asses of piety’ and
whose thorny resting-place is on the gimlet of the
Social Purity Society, and who is haunted bv
fierce Christians with darns in their language who
want to half-sole her morals. The unreal part
consists principally of the curate, who" has* a
tendency to deliver impassioned sermons to the
audience on every possible occasion, and some-
how this curate always brings down the
house, and his oiliest exhortation generally
fetches an encore. Miss Myra Kemble as
the Magdalen,, ought to be the principal attrac-
tion, but she isn’t. She plays the part in artistic
fashion—that is to say, she does it like a female
haunted by horrors and evil spirits, and after the
fashion of stage conspirators in general she sits up
violently at every falling leaf, and is so obviously
waiting for the deluge that only a particularly
blind old peeress could be taken in by her. If she
played it in any other fashion, however the
audience would probably rise at her with indigna-
tion, for it is a recognised fact that the stage
conspirator must always be known by the way his
or her hair stands up in constant apprehen-
sion all through three acts. Also Miss Kemble
throws off the conspirator, and rises bril-
liantly to the occasion on the only two occa-
sions where there is any occasion to rise
to. Mrs. Molyneux plays the part of Grace
Roseberry in the only way the character admits
of—as a hard, spiky British maiden of unblemished
ferocity ; but the public interest seems to centre
principally round Mr. A. R. Lawrence’s curate.
Why it should do so no one knows, for the curate
is unreal and long-winded, and there never was
and never will be such an individual until the
Established Church pales its ineffectual collection-
plate, and fades away. Mr. Lawrence saves the
healthy-looking preacher from being absolutely
ridiculous, but no man could do much more. The
long-lost Frank Cates does some good work as
Horace Holmcroft, the aristocratic Podsnap
of the drama; and Lady Janet Roy is
rendered by Miss Annie Taylor in a respectable
fashion which calls for no special comment.
The “ New Magdalen ” is a play which makes the
audience feel good, and there were sundry damp
spots in the dress-circle on Saturday night where
various impressionable females had been shedding
the tear of silent sympathy and thinking of the
Christian attitude they would have assumed if they
met the prodigal daughter in real life. Also one
stout gentleman was visibly affected. He owns two
terraces which are all let to “ unfortunates” at
rack rents, and he calls round like a devouring
pestilence every week for the money. Judging by
liis contrite appearance lie probably raised the rent
first thing on the ensuing Monday morning. “A

The “ Alabama Minstrels,”
at Sydney Garrick, keep improv-

oversets all the traditions of the
minstrel bu iness. This dangerous

confined to the Garrick, however,
and there are no special manifes-
tations of it at any other place of
amusement at present. The
Gyclorama and one or two othgr
solemn trifles of thesame ordercon-
linue on their track as heretofore,
and the smaller shows of the city
are still keeping out of the Benevo-
lent Asylum.
** * *
Madame Roth, of whom a
portrait is printed in this issue of
The Bulletin, arrived in N.S.W.
about seven years ago, intend-
ing to devote her time exclusively
to decorative art. Finding no
field in that direction, she turned
her attention to teaching, and with
great success. In spite of her time
being almost fully occupied by her
pupils, she was a constant ex-
hibitor at the current exhibitions.
Some time ago the trustees of
N.S.W. National Gallery pur-
chased a piece of her decorative
work, and in the water-colour
landscape competition last year
she was one of the five artists
whose work was chosen as illus-
trative of the scenery of the province. Madame
Roth has left cn route for New York, where she
hopes to find scope for her favourite pursuit,
decoration, and with this idea in view has pre-
pared a series of thirty or forty sketches of
Australian subjects, chiefly birds and flowers.
Madame Roth takes with her the good wishes of
many friends.

Mr. H. R. Jewett indulged in the luxury
of a benefit at Sydney Garrick on Monday, when
a programme was presented which was at least a
yard too long for the available space. The prin-
cipal features were one act of “ My Partner,” in
which the divine George Rignold appeared in the
character of Joe Saunders, one act of “Ihe
Judge ” by Myra Kemble and her company, and
an extract from the “ Corsican Brothers,” in all of
which Mr. Jewett took a hand. The rest of the
show consisted of fourteen different items, con-
tributed by Misses Jennie Lee, Emma Wangen-
heim, Viola Fortescue, Messrs. Howard "V ernon,
Geo. L. Gordon, Shine, Geo. Walton, and other
artists, who evidently meant well, and unduly
lengthened the show with no sinister intent.

»* * *
u The Silver King” enjoys a monopoly of
serious business at the Princess’, and Walter
Bentley shakes his snowy mane to the evident
satisfaction of large audiences. Also his perform-
ance of the name-part has grown somewhat
stronger at the knees and more harmonious than
at first. He still gets too drunk in the first act,
and too violently sober in the next, and too full of
ioyful hope in a resurrection when he finds that
he is dead. Put he comes back to England in
better condition and less like a mine-owner in
holy orders, so that the two halves of Wilfrid
Denver’s career seem to hang together by the
thread of the narrative. There are evidences of
a rising to higher things elsewhere. Sass and
Royce were excellent from the start, but George
Carey, as Father Christmas, has been able to fur-
nish and decorate his room for improvement during
the past week. Miss Watson, too, becomes a more
convincing Nellie Denver every night. In a quiet
sort of way Miss Watson is a better exponent of
this charming part than either of her predecessors,
who hadn’t such particular natural qualifications
for it. Corlesse gives a finished sketch of Cripps
the Cracksman, ' and Miss Gwynne reveals _ a
hitherto unsuggested depth of character in Susie,
the pretty waitress at “ The Checkers.” No other
Susie has betrayed such a splendid capacity for
loving a sailor, or so much womanly disappoint-
ment” anger, and disgust when the sailor doesn’t
reciprocate. This Susie is of infinite value to the
play, inasmuch as she makes silent confession that
Wilfrid Denver is the only case in which she has
ever known herself to fail. Her beauty sharpens
the point of the lesson. Everything is possible to
a man who refrains from kissing Susie.

The Bijou has been doing very well with
“Confusion,” and on Monday last the story of
the pug was supplemented by a local “skit,” in
one act, entitled “The Landslip.” If Brough,
made up after the image of Matthew Davies,
scores a success in this topical illusion it will
certainly be wanted again, and noticed in The
Bulletin anon. Meanwhile the B. and B. inten-
tion is to produce “A Village Priest,” five-act
drama by Sydney Grundy, on Saturday. This
play is said to be a howler, and anything in the
way of a substantial novelty may expect a cordial
reception under present circumstances.

At the Alexandra a new Australian play
has been engaged for Alf. Boothman’s benefit on
Friday, on which occasion the friends of A. P.
and the nobility and gentry in general will greatly
oblige by fighting for tickets. The late employes
of the Royal also think of taking a matinee bene-
fit at their former shop on Saturday, whilst Mr,
and Mrs. Royce are said to have similar intentions
as regards themselves. The term benefit has no
charitable flavour about it in this latter case, how-
ever. Royce doesn’t positively need another bene-
fit as far as anybody knows, and one cannot help
regretting that the still small voices of forgotten
favourites in distress should be drowned by the
personal appeals of prosperous artists,

The Rickavds-Coghill crowd have naturally
caught on at St. George’s Hall, because the show
is extra large of its kind. The Victoria is open
again under Slade Murray’s management, Mrs.
Dr. Ryder lectures at the Athemenm on a Von
Einkelatein system, and all the other places are
offering fresh attractions in the usual manner.
Newton Griffiths delivers lurid addresses on the
various figures at the Waxworks, and when ho
comes to the corpse of Deeming and tells the
excited throng all about the five-and- twenty
murders committed by the deceased monster, it is
well worth a shilling to be there and hear the dead
murderer libelled.

Mrs. George Darrell writes from London in ecstatic
praise of Patti and Arthur Roberts. The singing of the
mature diva paralysed her with its purity and ease, and,
as for Arthur Roberts—well—she thinks he’s ten times
funnier than all the Fred Leslies in the world. But,
judging by our remembrance of Mrs. Darrell, she would
scarcely be an authority on humour.

Rogers has positively, for the last time, parted for ever
from Minnie Palmer. “Yours Merrily,” says The Bulle-
tin’s correspondent, has invented a new seal which he

Connie Gilchrist, at her wedding with the Earl of
Orkney, really looked her best, says The Bulletin’s
London correspondent. She was dressed in grey-shot
corded silk with very wide sleeves of green velvet, and
epaulettes of lace which fell in graceful folds down to the
elbows. Her hat was a ravishing creation—fight straw
with a crinkled brim, and trimmed with lovely roses.
Altogether she looked most fetching, and well might her
very dear friend, the old Duke of Guiseberry, who gave
her away, look sad at the idea of parting with her. > There
were very few friends present. D’Arcy didn’t come
to see liis quondam flame (upon whom he lavished so
many diamonds) turned off. But D’Arcy is now too much
absorbed in politics to care for lovely woman. Besides,
he has a wife much older than himself who looks after
him. She is an Australian, and knows how to keep a man
in order. It is said that Connie’s dot was £30,000, which,
of course, was provided by Papa Beaufort.
A letter from Geo. W. Anson is jubilant with engage-
ments and rumours of engagements. Geo. W. has been
offered his pick of three scrumptious parts in
a comic opera to be shortly produced. Geo.
Barrett and our old friend “Billy” Elton are already
secured for the same production. Anson is also making
arrangements for a season of Eccles ac London Garrick,
and he has already disposed of tins American rights of a
comedy written by himself and Geo. Lash Gordon. A
London production of this comedy is, by the way, under
negotiation. Altogether the “Only” Anson is on a
gentle boom, and considers that nothing does an actor’s
standing so much good in the “Old Country as a suc-
cessful Australian sojourn. This, moreover, appears to
be true of all actors who have enjoyed good seasons in
the “ kawlinies”—all, at least, except Bellew.

stand that the Australian right in pieces belongs ex-
clusively to the managers who buy them. ‘ A Run of
Luck’ belongs to Bland Holt, just as ‘ The Gondoliers ’
belongs to J. C. Williamson, ‘ Dr. Bill ’ to Miss Myra
Kemble, and ‘Paul Jones’ to Miss Nellie Stewart. Mr.
George Rignold. therefore, has no chance of playing the
hero of any of these pieces even if his desires run in that
direction.’’’ George as the hero of a comic opera, or as
Dr Bill is a rather wild possibility, one must admit. But
it happened that he was specially engaged to appear in
the first production of “A Run of Luck ” at Melbourne
Royal. Wherefore, &c.
It is related that when Henry Herman, the dramatist,
was in the heyday of his success and was scooping in
about £2OO a week with the “Silver King” and other-
plays he was most extravagant in his expenditure and
used to give a dinner every Sunday which must have cost
close on £lOO. His friends remonstrated with him in
vain. “Aprbs moi le deluge,” was his reply. But Mrs.
Conover helped to cure him. On one occasion he was
tfivinof a specially grand spread—it was in January—and
he went to Covent Garden and bought three quarts of
new orcen peas at four guineas a quart. Everybody, cf
course, enjoyed them, especially Mrs. Conover, _ who
turned to Herman and said: “ Those ftre the finest tinned
veas I have ever eaten in my life, Mr. Herman. Where
do you get them ?” He never gave another dinner.

Editor Bulletin, —Dear Sir,—My friend, Dr. Neild, has
shown me something in The Bulletin about me. It says,
“Old Billy Hoskins left a baby son—what has become of
him?” 1 am that baby son, and I am in pretty good
health. I am glad to think my dear father is not for-
gotten, but I wish you would not call him “Old Billy
Hoskins.” Call him Mr. William Hoskins, the man who
loved Shakespeare and knew all about him. Please
excuse 1113’ saying this. lam at present staying with my
dear friend, Dr. Neild, who was my father’s friend and
who is my godfather.—l remain, dear sir, respectfully
yours, William Neild Baron Bowman Hoskins. Bilton
House, 21 Spring-street, Melbourne. August 29, 1892.
[The Bulletin stands reproved.— Ed. B.]

Walter Bentley’s “ Silver King ” ad. makes the common
error of misquoting the first line of canto 1 of “In
Memoriam.” The keynote of the whole poem is struck in
the line “ I held it truth with him who sings,” &c. Every-
one quotes it “I hold,” &c. The “him who sings,” it
may be added, is either Goethe or St. Augustine, or both.
The Wild Cat Column.
It looks much as if the swagger pretence of
resuming work at the Broken Hill mines was only an en-
deavour to rig the London share-market, after all. It was
plain enough to anyone except those who wouldn’t see it,
that the demonstration of recommencing work on Thurs-
day last was a fizzle, and that no operations on a scale
which would provide dividends enough to purchase a
dinner of cat’s-meat had been resumed. But, then, the
daily press was nobbled before-hand, and trumpeted the
return of a handful of shift-bosses and the firing up of a
12-horse-power engine for electric lighting, as if the mines
were all in full work. And the lie was cabled to England
in all its swelled splendour, and then the wretched Eng-
lishmen began to buy shares on the boom, and somebody
here got out from under, and the name of Australia will
stink worse than 50 bogus banks when the victims got to
know the game that has been played on them.
# * #
A S.M. Herald writer advises the Broken
Hill miners to go to work “ trusting to the public to see
that they receive fair remuneration.” Now', it is the
employers who refuse to leave the question of “ fair
remuneration” to the public’s representatives as pro-
vided by the Arbitration Bill.
* * *
Greta (N.S.W.) Colliery Co. has introduced
labour-saving coal-cutting machinery, which enables one
man to do two men’s work at one man’s pay, the
latter being fixed at 10s. a day, sixteen skips of
coal to be sent up early. The men offer to accept this,
provided the management finds powder and fuse. As the
machines represent a saving of Is. 9,1. per ton—the
difference between Is. lid. and 3s. Bd. per ton, or a
reduction in cost of nearly IS per cent.—the management
would still have the best of the new bargain.

The proposed Queensland Miniug-on-Private-
Property Act will probably, as a tangential result, further
strengthen an already powerful syndicate in the north of
that province. The private lands adjacent to the syndi-
cate’s property are in blocks of too small an area each to
be advantageously worked on “ lone hands.”

Dear Bulletin, —Apropos your first leader of
20th Aug. What paper is there w hich dares to demand,
in the interests of an insuring public, the same scrutiny
into life-assurance companies’ balance-sheets as is now
taking place with regard to those of land companies? If
the loading of assets' with unjustifiable increases in value
at a time of depression, and paying large dividends out
of deposits, is criminal on the part of a land company, it
is absolutely hellish to do so in a life insurance co., and
pay dividends on t of the premiums of thrifty ignoramuses;
vet the matter receives no comment, although it is the
talk of the town. Why ?-Old Hiram.

# * *
Since the appointment of the late N.S.W.
Parliamentary Draughtsman to the Land Court Presi-
dentship, two comparatively unknown men have been
appointed to draft Acts and watch their course through
committee. As showing the necessity of having compe-
tent men to do this work, recently-discovered anomalies
in the N.S.W. Friendly Societies’ Act are worth noticing.
Some of the building societies registered thereunder have
carried on business for years, under the blissful delusion
that the liability of their members was limited to the
amount of their shares. And now they are taking steps
to bring themselves under the Companies’ Act, with
limited liability. Hence the remarks made by Judge
Wilkinson at the annual meeting of that well-managed
institution, the N.S.W. Civil Service Building Society.
# * *
It is alleged in evidence that a certain recently
“broken” Sydney company used to charge interest on
many of its dead overdrafts at the rate of from 15 to 30 per
cent The interest wasn’t paid, as a igile, neither was the
overdraft hut even when they were both hopeless there
was comfort in sticking on the percentage. The case
seems very much like that of Walter Besant’s money-
o-rubbino- sailor, Stephen Cobbledick, who kept a book in
which he charged his penniless niece £2 a week for board
and lodging from her infancy, and used to present that
unhappy orphan at intervals with a bill for £lBOO or
thereabouts and urge an immediate settlement. And
when the old man got in a worse temper than usual he
would stick on compound interest till the wretched girl
almost wanted to be dead.
» * *
Almost daily the public are being told that the
formation of the Newcastle Coal Trust “ is progressing
satisfactorily.” The true position of tire alleged Trust is
p,is—a lot of small collieries who, without a combination
of this sort see their own collapse looming ahead, are
doin' 1- their utmost to get the big collieries to join. These
big collieries, like Wallsend, A.A. Co., Lambton, and two
or three others, have a full appreciation of their own
strength as well as of the others' weakness, and stand off.
Without them the Trust would be of precious little use,
and therefore it is not likely to eventuate.
# * *
Contrast in gold-mining. Of a mine at Tuena
(N.S.W.)it is reported : “Five tons of stone returned 28
ozs. of gold, or over five oz. per ton, and the tailings
assayed over 2 oz. per ton. The crushing proves this
mine to be a most valuable property.” Queensland:
“The manager of the True Blue, at Croydon, reports that
he has lifted several hundred ozs. from the plates, but
intends running through 1200 tons before cleaning up.”
# * •*
If newspaper-puffing would enrich mines, then
there would be two dividend-paying wonders at Dal-
morton, N.S.W., viz.—Mount Fool and Mount Pool
Marvel. Some twelve months ago these claims had one or
two small crushings of wonderfully rich ore. Short
shoots of good stone are a feature of the Halmorton
district; while the mints furnish employment for many
men, they never seem to return anything to shareholders.
# * *
After all the newspaper-puffing given to the
Mt. Poole Marvel, a crushing of 30 tons has, we see,
returned one oz. to the ton.

A big N.Q. mining syndicate provides in its
articles of association:—Any person who has become
entitled to a share by any other way than by transfer
may, instead of being registered himself, elect to have
some person, to be nominated by him, registered as the
holder of such share.” This should be convenient tor
Cabinet Ministers and members of Parliament who might
desire to have fingers in syndicate-pies without their con-
stituents knowing anything about it.

A Sunday paper has had a wonderful record as
a “ mining tipster.” In turn it has urged its readers to
buy Bonanza Cottsols, Sunny Corner, Mount Galena, Pain-
hulas, Mount Stewarts, and other well-known stocks with
great assurance and persistency. In nearly every inst ance
ih'j shares have fallen from pounds to shillings, shillings
to pence, and pence to nothing. Such luck is really piti-
able. Silent as the grave concerning its previous predic-
tions, it now wanls'to boom Bendigo stocks, which Heaven
help, when such a Jonah comes to their aid.
# # »
Old George Lansell is again getting the lion’s
share of the Bendigo dividends. It was the same twenty
years ago. Lansell limited his speculations to Bendigo
till the occurrence of the silver-boom, and returned to
Australia from England when it was at its maddening
pitch. He plunged into Barrier properties, and dropped
out a sadder and wiser man when he had parted with
some £50,000. He says he lias had enough of silver.
Lansell is probably the gamest and most enterprising big
gold-mining spectator in the world : he stands to his guns
with a fixed determination to make more money or lose
all he has acquired in mining. Had things not taken a
turn in Bendigo, he would probably have gone under
altogether— his losses during the last seven years might
be reckoned by hundreds of thousands. At present
he is getting it back at the rate of thousands a week.
He is now offering £5OO reward to anyone who will profit-
ably treat 100,000 tons of Bendigo tailings. Taken all
round, there is little gold left in Bendigo tailings, and
the process which can work them at a profit will n'»ke a
fortune for its inventor elsewhere,
# • *
The syndicate who built that excellent and well-
situated arcade, the Sydney Strand, are trying to float
the concern into a company, with a capital of £125,000.
The properly is all leasehold, hut we notice that the
amount of the ground-rent is not stated in the prospectus.
The prospects of the Strand may be very good, but The
Bulletin, which knows something about city building-
leases, doesn’t, as a rule, hanker after investments in
that line. And, by the way, are the Strand tenants all
satisfied with the' rents as set down in the company’s
prospectus ?
# * *
Matthew Davies was interviewed in London
about the lamented Mercantile Bank, and cheerfully ex-
plained his own entire freedom from all moral responsi-
bility : “ I was nominally on the board of the bank as well
as on that of several other institutions. I was asked not lo
withdraw my name from them because I was told it would
injure them ; but, as a matter of fact, since I took an
active interest in polities my connection with these
societies and companies has been more nominal than
formerly. I sat upon the boards, but 1 was not acquainted
with many of the transactions. You know very well that
the majority of these transactions are carried out by the
managing-directors.” Which practically amounts to
this; “I was paid to steer the ship. I kept the billet to
the last and drew the salary regularly, but latterly I was
asleep on the bridge all the time, so when the smash
came I really wasn’t responsible.” This reads welfc but
The Bulletin has known a man to get into serious trouble
before now for being asleep on the bridge.
* * *
Some more of the subtle mirthfulness of the
N.S. Wales mining industry. The Great Oohar Copper
Mine has, we believe, been shut down since August 1881).
The co. closed up with something over £4OOO in hand, and
carefully stowed away this amount to resume work with
when better times come. Lately, the shareholders have
found that instead of gathering interest the balance is fast
vanishing away, and upon enquiry it is explained that the
directors piopose to absorb £1875 for directors’ fees, Or
something like £5OO a year for sitting down and waiting
for copper to rise. At least it is hard to see what else, so
long as the mine is shut down, there is for a director to
do." At this rate, the shareholders should have calls made
on them presently.

Personal Items.
-Sir Matthew Davies is 42 years old.
Duncan Gillies was once a reporter on the
Ballarat Star.
The brother of an Irish Earl and English
Baron drives a cab in Sydney.
Brodribb, .Victorian Inspector-General of
schools, is a cousin to Henry Irving.
Sir G. Repub'ican Dibbs and Sir Matthew
■Davies return in the same boat. Omen?
One of the staunchest unionists in West
Queensland is the son of a prominent Judge.
The ablest Australian police-court lawyer is
the son of a judge whose name ho does not boar.
“ Price Waning ” has a work on Austra-
lian chronology now passing through the English
press.
From a paragraph in London World one
infers that the Princess of Wales is lo ing her
reason.
Lord Brassey is the owner of 40 miles of
land, almost entirely uncultivated, contiguous to
Perth (W.A.).
G. H. Reid, loader of the N. S. Wales
Freetrade party, went to Protectionist Melbourne
for his better-half.
Home Ruler John Dillon posed to a
popular sculptor as the Christ for an altar-piece
for a Melbourne church.
Bennett, brewer, Victorian M.L.A., says
the temperance leaders are either “ broke ” or in
gaol, or on the way there.
Of the present S.A. Ministry, Holder and
Handyside are the only ones who wear the emblem
of inner Toryism—a bell-topper.
Lord Hopetoun begins to feel sorry that
he learned to bark like a dog. He is always
having to do it to amuse the baby.
Dean Macartney ninety - three was
strolling about Melbourne one day last week, and
spoke at a meeting in the evening.
Griffith is always safe from the jingler.
No rhyme for his name—except “ sniffeth ” and
“ bitfeth,” which aren’t poetic, anyhow.
Lord Roberts, the Commander-in-Chief of
India, gets physically prostrated in the presence
of a cat. How does he feel about a Bear?
At Brisbane Union Club a waiter once
handed millionaire Tyson a dish of tripe. “No,
thanks, mister,” said James. “ I never eat offal.”

travelled in sewing-machines, once met a farmer
who refused to buy a machine because he sowed all
his wheat out of a bucket.

wh i has written some excellent stories for This
Bulletin, is reported from Brisbane as being in a
most serious state of health.
Gilbert, the bushranger, was in the
employ of the late N. 11. D. Bond, before taking
to the road, and wrote Bond a letter, thanking him
for his sound instruction in riding.

is the birth-place of some most conspicuous
literary notables : Shakespeare, “George Eliot,”
Walter Savage Landor and ’Enery Parkes.

land-grant railways.

is South-Australian-born.

another symptom. Now, Parkes

cabled, has written a letter to London Daily
News in support of the Kanaka slave-trad.*, i s
the proprietor of a boys’ school at Nundah, and is
f drly learned in the art of self-advertisement.
The Rinquets, who recently inherited the
I)e la Ruche millions, lived in a bark humpy near
Mackay(Q.). The daughter has only one optic,
and worked at house-to-house sewing, while the
son earned 10s. a week on a neighbouring planta-
tion.

this information. You don’t spell ‘ taters ’ with
a p /”
Paddy O’Sullivan, M.P. (Q.), says ho
knew a Queensland Minister who, when ho first
became M.P., used to buy tlie whole of his
speeches for one session for j£s. The writer knew
an Australian parson (once a r/uari-pressmnn) who
had “ Colonial sermons ” written for him at half-
a-crown apiece; five shillings, for any discourse
which took more than 20 minutes to reel off; a
shilling extra for three verses of original poetry
on any stirring event of the day.

in the friendliest way in his letter to the S.M.

knows, the impression left is that Keene was a
miserly curmudgeon.

Attorney-General of New Barataria.

That man of many sheep, William Halliday,

rich as is popularly supposed. When credited
w th driving one of the wool-drays through the
Sydney streets in that memorable strike-time pro-
cession, ho was almost as much under the whip
of capitalistic institutions as were his own rouse-
about®.

Alfred Deakin, unwarned by the example
of Munro, takes the chair at Melbourne teetotal
cackles, and listens to the sweet young matronly
logic of Mrs. Harrison Lee. This lady scorns the
thought of giving monetary compensation to pub-
Means for “doing wrong,” because Deeming, when
he did wrong, was “compensated by having a
rope put round his neck.” Mrs. Lee forgets that
Deakin defended the party in question, and that
the poor pub.-keepers may be “instinctive
criminals” who deserve pity instead of vengeance.
“ Tiger ” Inglis always boasts that he’ s
Scotch. “ I was Bst. 41b. when I camo to this
country,” said he, the other day, slapping his
embonpoint by way of emphasis ; “ now I’m over
10st., and all owing to good Australian beef and
climate.” How little of him is Scotch, after all!

One of the papers has published a long
article about “ fascinating murderesses,” but the
ones dealt with are all English. No mention is
made of the late Louisa Collins, the Sydney
husband-poisoner, who, ere she could be con victed,
brought about the disagreement of two juries by,
in each case, fixing at least one juryman with her
glittering eye.
“ Prayer-meetings at Newcastle (Eng.) to
beseech aid for the candidature of Mr. Morley.”
Fancy asking God to approve of Morley, the Agnos-
tic, who writes “God ” with a small “g ! ” But
these prayer-meetings were bound to fail, anyhow.
All the bishops and orthodox clergy are against
Gladstone, and if the Almighty pays any atten-
tion to dissenters there is an end to law—-and
order.
They say that the Queen jumped on her
Crown, and refused to be comforted, when her
son-in-law Lome was rejected at the Central
Bradford election by a large majority. The
unhappy Marquis is always bringing ignominy
upon the Royal Family, and the Queen regards
him as a danger to the Constitution. Some years
ago, when they put him on horseback amongst a
procession of kings and princes, he disgraced his
relatives by falling off.
Mrs. Slight, a very old identity, has died
at Rangitikei (M.L.) after suffering for more than
half a century from hope deferred. She was the
sole heiress to a large fortune which the British
Court of Chancery consistently declined to part
with. The lady’s great-grandfather died last
century, leaving his fortune to his son, who was
understood to be somewhere on the Continent.
The son was never again heard of, and until his
death is proved the Court won’t shell out. If he
were alive he would now be about 130 years of
age, but even if he would be 1100 years old the
Court wants documentary evidence ere it recog-
nises his decease. Two generations of Slights
have waited patiently for the lost coin, and a
third one is now waiting.
There is a man up North who cut a tir
weathercock to look like Sam Griffith, and fixed
it on his chimney. On changeable days, when the
wind did not know which way to blow, he would
shake his fist at it and say ; “ Torn, you wretch
turn 1 you can’t turn like he does.” The other day
a gale blew the weathercock down ; but the owner
would not put it up again. lie says it is a good
omen.
James Gordon, S.M., new Adelaide police-
magistrate, is a brother of J. H. Gordon, Minister
of Education in (he Ministry which appointed
him. “Jack ” Nicholson, the new Port Adelaida
magistrate, familiarly known in Adelaide as “Old
Nick,” claims Playford, leader of the Opposition
as his brother-in-law. In each case the claims of
ill-paid and long-service country magistrates were
disregarded.
The new British Secretary of State for the
Colonies, the Marquis of Ripon, belonged—he
was then a lord—to the Society of Christian
Socialists founded years ago by Charles Kingsley
Thomas Hughes (author of “Tom Brown’s School-
days”) Maurice, and, we think, Clough, thepoet.
It was probably owing to his Socialism that
Ripon’s Indian Yiceroy-ship gained him such
popularity with the natives and so large a portion
of dislike from the livery, peppery officialdom that
lives by suction in that appanage of the Crown.
“Old” George Thornton’s friends, writes
our London correspondent by last mail, will be
pleased to hear that he has recovered from the
effects of the painful operation he lately under-
went, and is now fast regaining his old healthy
looks. He leaves London on Tuesday next, and
has sent liberal donations to the hospitals by way
of thanks-offering. An ancient crony of Thorn-
ton’s, once a well-known man in Sydney, but now,
I suppose, almost forgotten, is still to the fore-
Charlie Newton, who of yore was the head and
front of everything in the shape of sport in
Sydney. He bears his years well, for he is no
chicken. Talking of George Thornton reminds
me that he was once upon a time quite a per-
former in the pigskin, and rode several races at
the Drapers’ meeting at Homebush, which, about
35 years ago, was one of the most notable N.S.W.
reunions.
Some time ago a certain member of London
Savage Club, noted for his economy of the truth
was telling some astounding yarn and vouching
for its absolute accuracy. Old Odell listened
awhile in amazement, and then said to the waiter:
“Go to the library, please, and bring me a Bible.”
The waiter departed and duly returned with the
sacred volume. Odell handed it to Christie
Murray, asking him (as he could not see very well)
to turn to the Acts of the Apostles, naming chap-
ter and verse. This proved to be the story of
Ananias and Sapphira. “Read on,” said he.
Christie did so until he came to the words “ And
Ananias went forth.” “Stop there,” said Odell ;
“now, why did Ananias go ‘forth?’” Nobody
could answer. “He went fourth,” said Odell,
“because , and —— , and (naming
three well-known club liars who were present)
went first, second, and third.”
Mesfcon tells this yam himself to illustrate
the all-pervading character of fame:—“l was
walking down Queen-street not long ago when a
man stopped me. I had never seen him in my
life before. He said: ‘Excuse me, sir, but are
you Mr. Meston?’ I admitted that I was. ‘Yes,’
he said ; ‘ but, pardon me, sir, I mean are you Mr.
Meston, the great explorer?’ ‘Well—yes, 1 sup-
pose so,’ I said. Then he said : ‘ I want you to
do me a great favour, Mr. Meston.’ ‘What is
it ?’ I asked him. ‘ltis a very great favour, Mr.
Meston,’ he said - ‘I wish —that is, I would like—
if you—if you would condescend to shake hands
with me.’ ‘Certainly, my man,’ I said, and
shook. He wrung my hand with effusion and
said : ‘Thank you, sir ; thank you, Mr. Meston.
Now I shall go home and tell my wife and family
that I have shaken hands with Mr. Meston, the
great explorer.’ Now, wasn’t that curious?”
adds Meston, looking round—“never saw the man
in my life before.”
Review of Reviews--Shadow of a Shade.
The Irish population lias sunk 750,000 in
the last decade.
Dear Bulletin, —Under “Plain English,”
in “The Caucasian—Played Out?” you remark that
Syrians, Afghans, and Sikhs go “ on the wallaby”
with a bundle of odds and ends, and make more
than wages. Undoubtedly they do, but don't
you remember Tom Scott, who took to tumbling
for a living after the death of Quilp? Tom
couldn't make the game pay until he assumed the
name of the Italian image-boy. Reason the rest
out. -Young ’Un.
A Labour agitator, writes a correspondent,
visited the Percy River, Q., the other day and
wanted to know where he was going to speak. A
waggon was pointed out to him alongside a
crushing mill ; but it would be impossible, ho
said, to make himself heard. “ Oh, we'll stop the
mill while the meeting’s on,” said the boss. “My
men want to hear yon, and so do I.” How sweet
is harmony between Labour and Capital! Just
fancy the Southern boss stopping his mill to let
the boys bear an agitator !
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co.’s “Cascade,’
80 and 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is
just the place ; it is certainly the largest and best-
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*

phesies that the Dibbs-Barton Government will

party Government should be abolished.” The
question of elective executives looms larger every
day on the horizon of practical politics.
So “ Sir George Grey’s Bill to make
Governors elective is calculated to frighten British
investors.” All right; let us have Australia
governed by a parliament of British investors.

“Kokum ” Epps, for the National Ass., is
flooding the N.S.W. country press with essays on

“ Kokum ” and Co. isn’t “ self-government.” They
want to govern everybody else.

Wise “ a man above the ordinary,” and says time
will cure his superfluity of irritability. So ! but

Wise’s selfishness and insincerity.

By refusing to repeal the Superannuation
Clause of the Queensland C.S. Act (after giving
two distinct and definite promises to that effect)

Stirling, M.L.C., who pretends to be an
opponent of payment of members, has just
accepted the post of Chairman of Committees in
tl.o S.A. Council at £4OO a year. The Council
sits for five months in the year, two days a week,
and about three hours a day, while it is only in
Committee about a third of that time. His re-
muneration, therefore, is at the rate of about £lO
an hour.

The present Maori-
land Parliament is the
biggest, measured by the average length of its
members, which has ever sat in Wellington. It
contains twelve men each over six feet high, while
the longest previous Parliament only contained
nine. Also, it is about the brainiest Legislature in
Australasia, despite the theory that long men are
devoid of intelligence. Probably it was the envious
short men who started this theory. The malignity
of a very short man who is baulked of a Govern-
ment billet is a horrible tiling.
Owing to a disagreement between some
little niggers belonging to Dutch New Guinea
and a few small aborigines in the British section
of the same island, Sir William Macgregor lias
asked Governor Norman to ask the British
Government to ask the Queen of the Netherlands
to suppress the lawlessness prevailing at the
Dutch end of the colony. Then the matter will
be referred, no doubt, to the Governor of Dutch
New Guinea, and he will ask his sovereign to ask
the British Government to ask Governor Norman
to tell Macgregor that the matter shall bo
attended to.
N. S. Wales contains 195,882,150 acres ;
45,731,9GI acres, or 23 per cent., are alienated, or
in process of being alienated ; 132,425,324 acres
or 07 per cent., are leased ; 17,724,502 acres, or
about 10 per cent., are held by the State in rail-
ways, roads, reserves, &o. Of the 45,731,904 acres
alienated, 21,283,393 acres, or nearly 47 per cent.,
were sold in fee simple ; 2,084,112 acres, or about
5 per cent., by deferred payments ; 3,123,094 acres,
or about 7 per cent., wore free grants ; the balance,
about 42 per cent., is still “ going, going.” Of the
132,425,024 acres of leased lands, the pastoralists
have 58,410,794 acres, or about 44 per cent.; occu-
pation licenses, 40,038,749 acres, or nearly 38 per
cent.; conditional leases, 11,234,131 acres ; home-
stead licenses, 8,005,035 acres.
Under the new cast-iron Standing Orders
of Queensland (intended to choke the Labour-
party), a member adjudged guilty of contempt
may lie lined up to ,£5OO, and kept under arrest,
or in confinement, until the fine is paid, or until
end of the session. He has to give the
Sepjeant-at-Arms £lO for arresting him, pay that
official ss. per mile for conveying him to his parti-
cular “jug,” and also pay £2 per day in fees for
every day he is kept under arrest. If, for in-
stance, a Labour-member were fined £5OO at the
commencement of a session, he couldn’t possibly
pay it, and by the end of the session—say, about
150 days—he would owe £BOO, besides “ mileage
fees,” and could probably be made insolvent, or
otherwise forced out of Parliament.
Governor Jersey (N.S.W.); “It does not
detract from a Governor’s position that he holds
the office of Grand Master of Freemasons in New
South Wales.” If that be so, if it doesn’t detract
from a Governor’s position that he is head of a
(pretendedly or otherwise) secret society, then
nothing can detract from a Governor’s position.
It doesn’t detract from a Governor’s position to
be Worthy Grand Mongrel of the Vermilion
Order of Yellow Pups. It doesn’t ditto to be
Worthy Chief Washtowel of the Loyal Order of
Grovelling Huckabacks. It doesn’t likeioise to be
Boss Proboscis of the Postdiluvian Guild of the
Illuminated Oriflamme. It doesn’t, in fact,
matter a Chioago-Dibbser whether an Imported
Figurehead is the Butt-end of the Goat, or the
Goat-end of the Butt—or anything—or nothing.

A puzzled West Queensland Acting-C.P.S.,
a police-sergeant, asked to issue a summons
against a drover for not travelling his stock the
regulation day’s distance, wired to Brisbane to the
Chief Inspector for information. The reply came
(unpunctuated, of course); “Act relating to
travelling stock see. 55 cattle 6 miles sec. 58 sheep
fi miles.” “ Ye-e-s, ’ remarked the C.P.S., scratch-
ing his skull-thatch, “ye-e-s, that’s all right, but
he gives the number of (scarlet) stock that have to
travel the G miles !”
The disadvantages attached to the country-
newspaper habit of issuing cheap-and-nasty sup-
plements (supplied wholesale by city printing firms
at Chinese rates), consisting mainly of pirated
matter and literature paid for at “ sweaters’ ”
prices, has been strikingly illustrated in the case
of a Bathurst (N.S.W.) journal, some of whose
rivals have been quoting from one of its “supple-
ments,” an article diametrically opposed to those
political and party views which it has been all
along sledge-hammering into its constituents.
Dear Bulletin,— The existing Charter®
Towers papers are bound to the chariot-wheels of a
capitalistic clique. Their reports of the Day
Dawn trouble arc fair samples of their onesided-
ness. By the way, touching that same, the
working miners were badly exasperated at the
refusal of the P.M. to grant Vosper bail, and on
the Thursday and Friday nights they formed
processions of over 1000 strong, marched up to the
lock-up and cheered for “ prisoner ” Vosper. Then
they hooted the local press and mining-manager
Rollston. The police-sergeant wired to Towns-
ville for additional assistance, and six constables
were sent along, armed with revolvers, &e., but
had the miners been inclined to be rough—well !
A movement is afoot to start a workers’ daily
paper on the Towers. About .£5OO is already pro-
mised. and more than half of that has "been
actually paid in. Numbers of men are daily
joining the Associated Workers’ Union. Wo will
yet return Vosper to Parliament.—Yours, Miner,
Charters Towers, 30. July, ’92.
Newspaper accounts are to hand of the
decapitation at Foochow (China) of one of five
pirates, recently captured in the Hainan Straits.
The dead man’s liver was divided between the
three executioners, fried in his caul fat and eaten,
A condemned man’s liver thus taken is reckoned a
certain cure for consumption, and also a talisman
against his spook haunting the law-finishers.
A man who has travelled all over Queens-
land during the last two years gives this as his
downright opinion:—“Don’t you make any
mistake about Bananaland, when you hear it’s
broke. Present insolvency—and a cold outlook
for at least three years—rests like a heavy fog
upon Brisbane, but upon Brisbane only. All the
rest of Queensland is commercially prosperous.
And after three years more of Purgatory, there
will be a boom in Brisbane too.”
A settler on a desolate sand-patch in a
dismal, ghost-strewn, spectre-haunted region near
the Great Australian Bight claims to own
Leichardt’s long-lost mule which is flourishing to
this day. He is alleged to have been found at
some indefinite place in the far interior with an
aboriginal legend attached to him to the effect
that he was one of a party which travelled into
the interior some (150 odd moons ago. Therefore,
the animal is understood to be GO years old, on a
rough estimate, which rather corroborates the
story than otherwise, for heaven and earth may
pass away but the mule dieth not, but goeth on
for ever; and when the end of the world comes,
the chances are. that the last man will be a mule,
and it is quite as likely to be Leichardt’s mule as
any other.
In the early days of Adelaide, just after
the arrival of a shipload of Prussian political
exiles, an aboriginal entered a draper’s shop in
the city and said ; “ You gib it tschirt; all same
white man’s money,” and he produced three florins
and a sixpence. Having chosen the shirt ho
wanted, Jacky suddenly proceeded to divest him-
self of the one lie had worn for the previous
year, and to don the new garment. The opera-
tion was watched with much awe, especially by
the shop-girls and their lady customers. “ Now
you gib it paper,” said Jacky. “ What for ?” was
the salesman’s query. “Wrap up long a tschirt,”
was the reply. “Oh,” said the draper, “you
don’t want that dirt}', greasy thing. Leave it
here and I’ll burn it.” The careful nigger caught
hold of the garment, and answered—“My word,
no fear; you gib it paper. Me gib um" tschirt
poor plurry German.”
Thus a correspondent :
Dear Bulletin, —Does any Lodging-House Act obtain
in Sydney? lam poor, but up to date of cleanly habits,
and I, the other night, rented a shilling bed, right in the
centre of the city. I was shown up two flights of stairs
into a room with a sloping ceiling, the lowest part of
which was about four-feet high. In this attic were four
beds, of three of which J was allowed 1113' choice. How-
ever, as it turned out, I had all the room to myself. But
had three other customers turned up, and supposing that
(like myself) Ihey all smoked, what an atmosphere we
should have breathed! There were one wash-stand,
one basin, no jug at all, no water (I suppose shilling-
dossers in the city never wash on these cold mornings),
and one jack-towel (utterly useless under the circum-
stances). Many months ago, I slept one night at a
Paddington establishment that accommodates men by
the dozen, and when I asked to be shown to the bath-
room, I was stared at as a curiosity. A crowd came to
gape at the man who expected to find a bathroom in such
a place. If there is no Lodging-House Act in force in
Sydney, I trust some lion, member will introduce a Bill
into Parliament to reform lodging-houses.— Countryman.
When Doctors Disagree!
Professional etiquette almost always prevents
one doctor finding fault with another’s treatment,
no matter how erroneous he may consider it, but a
case where they quite differed occurred the other
day in the city of Melbourne. A poor, nervous
patient, suffering from the follies of early life,
called upon a medical man, who subjected him to
a thorough examination and then, putting on his
most sympathetic and serious look, exclaimed,
“My poor man, you are in a terrible and
dangerous condition, your spinal cord is only
hanging by two threads, and it will cost one
hundred and fifty guineas to cure you.” This
announcement of course frightened the patient out
of a year’s life, and he considered he had better
have another opinion if the case was so serious,
and went to another doetor, who also examined
him carefully and pronounced “Dr.' is a
fool, for your spinal cord is only hang-
ing by one thread and it will cost three hundred
guineas to cure you.” And he got it. So much
for frightening nervous subjects, three hundred
guineas for a few bottles of physic not half so good
or reliable as Clements Tonic. This medicine
will cure the worst case of nervous debility when
all the quacks have failed to “ restore manhood
a course always results with success, but it must
not be expected that one bottle will give any man
a new lease of life. If your case is worth treating
or your life valuable to you, treat the case
thoroughly and faithfully and you will meet’with
the same success as others do. H. H. Bestman,
Esq., Klein-stieet, Toowoomba, Q., writes :
“Knowing that you are always pleased to hear of
the cures effected by your remedy, I am glad to
state that I have derived great results by its use.
Some years ago 1 caught a severe cold which
induced dyspepsia, severe pains all over my body,
the joints being swollen, my appetite impaired and
a great swelling under my right arm, all finally
ending in St. Vitus’ dance, my body being con-
tinually ‘on the shake.’ 1 consulted a physician ;
he prescribed medicines, hot baths and rubbing
the body with Rhus. Tox, and so on; but his
treatment did me no good. He then had recourse
to Clements Tonic and said I must take a course
of that medicine. I did so, and so recovered my
appetite, and my strength began to improve
generally, and in eight months the shakes were
entirely stopped, and now I am entirely cured,
solely by the use of Clements Tunic." Patients
must be on their guard against imitations ; insist
on having what you call for, “Clements Tonic.”
Refuse all where tiie package has been tampered
with. F. M. Clements, Melbourne and Sydney.

THE SUPPRESSION OF
LARRIKINISM.
A number of regulations have been drafted
by the Minister of Justice in connection with
Ins bill for the supression of -disorderly cOn.
duct. He is by na means prepared to make
residence in gaol a pleasant change for
characters of the larrikin type, and his re.
gulations are consequently as vigorous as
present day views of punishment will per*,
mit. While the health of tho prisoner ia
gaol for brutal assaults and conduct generally
"associated with “pushes” will not be
neglected,-the Minister proposes to make big
stay in prison as little to be regarded with
complacency as it is .possible to do. The
principal hardship will be In the restricted
diet, which is literally In certain cases to
consist of bread and water, and nothing else.
In every sentence under the bill for a period
under 10 days this frugal diet is to be pro-
vided ; when the sentence is for a longer
period the diet drill be mainly bread and
water, but other lood is to be allowed
under the orders should it be deemed
necessary for the preservatidaof health. The
sleeping accommodation is also not to be too
luxurious. What is known as the "plank”
bed is to be insisted on'for the whole terra of
imprisonment. "

Society
A lady, born and educated in Australia,
was lately travelling on a mail-steamer in Japanese
waters, and entered into a protracted conversation
with the stewardess, a German-American. “ I
can’t for the life of me make out,” said the latter,
“what country you come from. You aren’t a
’Murkan, and I’m sure you’re not English, because
you don’t speak good English.” “What do you
mean ?” asked the lady—“ do you mean that you
never have heard me say anything like ‘Steyoo-
ahdess, bring me a glass of wahtah, please ! ’ ”
“Waal, yes,” said the stewardess, “ I guess that’s
what I did mean ” “ And which English do you
prefer—the ‘good English’ or my English?”
“ Waal, mum,” returned the other, “ I guess 1 like
your English because I can understand it.”
* * *
If Australia isn’t “Greater Britain,” it is
at least “Greater London.” Even tolerably well-
educated Australians, in pronouncing words con-
taining the long-a vowel, fall into a branch of
Cockney twang, as, for instance, pronouncing
“lady” as though it were spelt “lydy.” At a
certain Sydney down-the-harbour picnic most of
the revellers went ashore near a skating-rink. One
girl, being asked why she lingered aboard the
steamer, explained : “ I don’t skite.” Later, when
refreshments were being served, someone asked :
“ What’s Nelly doing ?” “ Oh, she’s looking after
the plttes (plates).” Again : “Kite, will you have
some rtsons?” Kate : “No, thanks ; I’ve got some
gripes !” Then the chief mite choked himself with
Aike.
• • *
A meeting for the Promotion of Public
Morality was held in Melbourne last week, and if
any unregenerate cynic wandered into the gather-
ing by accident, he probably took a back seat and
smiled vigorously. At the outset, the venerab’e
Dean stuck himself up, so to speak, with the
Scriptures, where lie “found that a man was
allowed to have more wives than one, while a
woman was only allowed to have one husband.”
After this the meeting seemed to find the Scrip-
tures impracticable, and left them severely alone.
Discussion drifted on to the necessity for protect-
ing infant life, without resorting to tho awful
immorality of a foundling hospital, but here again
were believers in the sacredness of the marriage-
tie set pondering over an unc:ngeuial fact. One
moralist mentioned that, “ with certain excep-
tions, the vast proportion of illegitimate children
were the healthiest children born.” The absurdity
of taking any measures, direct or indirect, to
further reduce tho physique of the rising genera-
tion was now quite apparent, so the meeting fell
back upon the eternal question of organisation,
and finally departed, with a sad conviction that
the present state of public morality was all owing
to the want of a paid secretary.
* # *
The bursting of the boom lias certainly
cleared the social atmosphere of N.S. Wales and
Victoria. The Pharisees who infested every
“ V.K.” public and private function, and postured
prominently on the dais ; the loudly-dressed snobs
whose vogue it was to welcome every more tban-
ordinary visitor “ in tho name of Australiaand
the greasy saints whose eyes were the whitest,
whose public graces were tho longest, whose
prayer-meeting groans were the awfnllest, and
whose coins dropped in the missionary-boxes like
stirrup-irons on a steel floor; these and a lot more
pinchbeck pretenders have scurried away to their
secret holes, and there is now a chance of occa-
sionally seeing and hearing a few healthy and
honest people move and talk.
# * •
The Bulletin finds no fault with Gover-
nor Ilopetoun for discussing the usefulness of
Clydesdale entires at an agricultural • show
luncheon, and in the presence of ladies. We
would like to hear somebody lecture on the breed-
ing of mankind at a baby-show. When women
once begin to understand that a healthy, intelli-
gent human sire does as much for the good of the
country as a well-selected stud-horte, the influence
of the gilded, rickety dude on posterity will grow
beautifully less.
# # •
Looked at from any point of view, the
alleged “Keely Cure” for drunkenness has a
fishy aspect. It is highly alcoholic, to start with.
It is a quack remedy whose virtues mostly consist
in the loudness and frequency and cheekiness of
the quacking. It is as greedy and shindykitish a
business and advertising spec, as any Rubemout’s
soap, or Horsetail’s hair-restorer, or crystalline
competition lotion, or universal pill, or Salvation
Army. It refuses to submit itself to unchallenge-
able tests by recognised medical authorities. Its
main strength is alleged to ,be its secrecy. It is
taken up and boomed by physicians who indi-
vidually, and apart from specious and cunning
advertising, are medical failures. Its other chief
allies are hollow-headed posturing parsons who
tove to mount on any emotional wave-crest.

with the Rev. Howling Yawper type of platform-
parson, the aroma of tho scheme is tew
mutch.

A well-dressed youth one recent morning
engaged rooms at a small hotel, paying cash in
advance. In the afternoon, he had retired to Ins
bedroom, when his door was stormed by some
fussy domestics who imperatively insisted that
“ the woman you've got in there” must be at once
thrust forth. In vain he avowed that the lady
was his wife ; the servants gave him no quarter.
So he sent for the hotel manager, who, for some
reason or other, opined that the youth was not
married at all, and that such goings-on, you know
—his shoulders, almost following his eyebrows to
tlie ceiling, finished the sentence. The cause of
all the rumpus shrieked to be taken away from tho
horrid place; then she fainted and finally had to bo
half carried off the premises. Since then the
lawyers have begun to take an interest in that
hotel manager and his amateur Gimlet Brigade.

A certain holy young man, who used to
evangelise the Chinese in an Australian city,
not long ago, met an attractive wanton there—a
Jewess, the mistress of a boss Chow. The wily
woman angled for that green young man, and
married him. That’s it. Alas for the emotional
youth who tries to reclaim the strange woman !
# * #
By the way, not long ago a philanthropic
Melbourne journalist went to a lot of trouble to
rescue a fallen sister, lie seemed to persuade the
female of the error of her ways, and she repented
with great gusto. But just as the zealous press-
man was beginning to hug the idea that all was
well with his protegee, she wandered back to
Little Bourke-street, complaining bitterly that he
had never once tried to hug her. She had expected
to be given fresh chances to repent, every now and
then.

The Catholic Times (Wellington, M.L.)
gets up on its spiritual ear ;
After offering the perfectly magnilcont. sum of £lO for
a design for his Australian (not Australasian, it will he
observed ; Stead’s geography being weaker than his
blazing morality) edition of the Review of Reviews, Stead
has got his design, and is only paying £5 for it, owing to
some artistic “improvements” which he has himself
introduced. It looks like a £5 design. On the top
corners are portraits of Stead and Fitchett —the latter
being the Australian editor; between them is a large-
sized, five-pointed star from which proceed rays. There
are more rays thrown on Stead than on Fitchett. The
two editors are in the centre of life buoys, which may
possibly be horse-collars, and are tied together with some-
thing which may he (he feeler of an octopus or a second-
hand wooden comforter with a bullock’s heart in the
centre. Beneath Stead and Fitchett, who are perched in
space, looking down upon the north pole, are stars,
comets, clouds and things, the word ninepence, and two
swollen periwinkles, also a black badge hearing the
legend “ Review of Reviews.” Then there is something
round with “ Australia ” on it. This round thing, Which
is probably a football—in delicate compliment to Aus-
tralian tastes—is supported on one side by a rhinoceros in
ship’s slings all ready to be hoisted on board something,
and on the other tya mouse with rabbit’s ears, standing
on its hind legs. These structures rest upon thistles and
other garden produce, which, in their turn, repose on
a clean hook with a white swan swimming about it. The
design is perfectly unique and is everyway worthy of the
good sense and profound artistic taste of that modest
creature, W. T. Stead.

A Launceston (Tas.) solicitor was “set
back ” the other day. lie appeared for a client
who sued for the price of a lady’s dress. The
defence was that the garment was ill-fitting—-in
fact, that a hiatus appeared between the skirt and
bodice that might suit a Hindoo, but not a ’Conta.
The magistrate, with the practical wisdom of a
Haroun-al-Raschid, directed the lady to put on
the dress. Attorney Clarke noted the ungainly
result, and asked the wearer if she had not
relaxed her stay-lace. Replied she promptly :
“Come and feel for yourself.” Poor Clarke col-
lapsed—really and truly men are ever so much
more modest than women. Verdict for defendant,

In a fashionable Sydney restaurant appears
this notice : “ Anyone not receiving proper atten-
tion is requested to report to the manager.”
Another notice should be placed beside it, thus :
“Any waitress receiving improper attention is
requested to report to the manager.” Then the
girls and well-dressed cads would have an equal
show. When we abolish barmaids, let us also
abolish waitresses and coffee-palace chamber-
maids. Better, in fact, abolish women altogether.
# * *
A Sydney barber lately went mad. He’d
have a man half shaved, and then suddenly hold
the razor aloft, and fix his stony gaze upon the
ceiling. The wretch in tho chair would then
perceive that he was at the mercy of a crank.
Some men would spring out of the chair and
knock the lunatic endways and then dash out into
the street hatless and eoatless and with one cheek
covered with lather ; others would sit there petri-
fied for half-an-hour, with the perspiration trick-
ling down into their boots. The poor crank ia
now in Gladesvillc.
* # *
A half-caste lady in Maoriland, who on
the Maori side can trace back her ancestors for
20 generations, is suing a local steamship company
for damages. She took a saloon passage from
Lyttelton to the Chatham Islands, and when she
got on board was blandly informed that she
would have to go steerage because the grocer’s
wife, and the butcher’s female, and the “missus”
of the land-grabber, and so on, refused to associate
with her by reason of her olive skin, and because
she wasn’t Hinglish. As she declined to go
steerage the captain returned her part of her
passage-money and put her ashore. It is a cheer-
ful thing to reflect that if the Christian’s dark-
skinned Asiatic Redeemer came back to earth now
the butcher’s wife would probably draw the
colour-line at Him also.
In N.S.W. Supreme Court recently a
newly-fledged barrister, son, too, of a remarkably
clever Judge, was endeavoring to cross-examine a
shrewd business man. Ho bad a heap of pass and
cash-books in front of him and was hopelessly
floundering amongst the figures when he received
some apparently great idea from the attendant
attorney and with austerity said: “Witness, did
you sell Mr. Hotelkeeper a consignment of
brandy?” “ Yes.” “ Did be pay yon ?” “Yes.”
“ Now be careful! where is the receipt he gave
you for the payment ?” Roars of laughter.

When an Australian male or female student
passes a “brilliant examination ’ at the Apothe-
caries’ Hall, London, why on earth should any
fuss be made about it in the press or elsewhere ?
The medical examinations at Melbourne and Syd-
ney Universities are infinitely tougher on the
average than the Scotch or English exams. As a
matter of fact, about half the students who leave
Australia to get qualifications abroad are afraid of
or have proved themselves unable to negotiate the
local scholastic fences.

When fairest Eve in Eden rose
From sleeping Adam’s side,
O Lord, Thou ledst Thy precious gift
To Adam for a bride:
So now Thine handmaid here bestow
On this, Thy waiting son ;
Unite them both in holy bonds,
A loving race to run.
A SONG IN THE NIGHT.
By the river the winds are sighing
Strange music of things undying,
The bamboos rustle and rave the whole night long.
The plaint of the fair flowers growing
Is sad, for the reaping and sowing
Cease not, and the Voice of Night is a careful song.
The free of tho dark stream, flowing,
Is troubled by breezes, blowing
From never unto forever in endless throng;
And phantoms, wildly flying
Hither and thither, are crying
Hasten, oh Dawn ! for Night is the mother of wrong.
Brisbane.
SHAHO.
AN UNORTHODOX WAIL.
Oh ! I want to go back to the city,
Away from this desolate place
With its acres of “ solitudes awesome ”
And the horror of “infinite space.”
I am tired ot “ the wail of the plover,”
l am sick of “ the magpie’s sweet song
I loathe “ the complaint of the curlew,”
I’ve heard it too oft and too long.
“ Long tramps thro’ the forests ” are failures,
“ Summer strolls by swift streams ” apt to pall;
I am weary of “fresh air and freedom,”
Since a surfeit I’ve had of them ail.
The sight of “ a drover” is deadly,
The “crack of his whip” drives me mad,
The low of wild cattle’s depressing,
And “ the bleat of the sheep” quite as had.
“ Misty mountains may tow’r in the distance,”
And soulful ones rave of their height;
1 wonder how far they’re from Sydney
If from them the city’s in sight.
The “ soil may he rich ” and the cattle
Exactly “ the thing ” in their breed ;
I wish they were mine and I’d sell them
And make for that city with speed.
A telegraph-wire and a sparrow
Furnish plenty of Nature for mo,
And a walk to save ’bus-fare sufficcth
To give me a relish for tea.
A sirloin on Sundays, or saddle
Of mutton’s enough of (heir kind
To prime me in “ good points in cattle ”
And such, once I’ve left these behind.
I yearn for the roar of street traffic.
For the howl of the newspaper-boy,
While the yell of the man with bananas
Is a dream of delirious joy.
The hell-birds may chime for the poets,
1 pine for the shriek of a tram ;
And “ the rustle of leaves in the autumn”
May be music—to me it’s all sham.
Brickfield Hill or, say, William-street (Upper)
Are “ mountains ” enough to please me,
And for “soil ” give me wood-blocks and pavements,
“ Rural streams ” the green hay near the Quay.
For oh ! there are hearts in the City !
There are souls! there are welcoming eyes!
And I long for a sight of my fellows,
For a word from the friends that I prize.
M. T.

When are Spectacles Inquired ?
When the eyes water or become so much fatigued by
short exercise as to be obliged to be frequently closed or
relieved by viewing different objects.
When objects cannot be seen without removing them
to an increased distance.
When the letters of a book appear to blend with one
another.
When more light is necessary than was formerly, as
When the candle is placed between the eye and the
book ;
When much difficulty is found in threading a needle ;
When the eyes appear as if they had a mist before
them;
When black specks seem floating in the sight.
When any of the above indications arise, all affectation
should be laid aside, responsible and skilful opticians con-
sulted, and a pair of preservers purchased.
PEOST & SHIPHAM
(Practical Spectacle Makers),
462, 474 and 587 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY,

A Dapto (N.S.W.) divorce co-respondent,
who seems to have badly mismanaged bis amours,
was cast in ,£250 damages in Sydney last week.
He was a victim to the old ruse of the absent
husband’s sudden return. He ran out of the house,
with the husband in full chase ; swam a creek;
hubby did ditto; tried to double back to the
house, but the other fellow got there first and
impounded his clothes. “ \Vhich I have them
yet,” he triumphantly informed the Court. Decree
nisi.
* * *
What may happen to a husband in N.S.W.
Seven years ago a couple (both under age) married,
and lived together for nine months, when the wife
skedaddled ; eight months later she returned to
her home and hub ; five months later still she again
left him and openly lived immorally; another five
months elapsed and she was taken back again.
This see-saw connection continued until —whoop
fur the sex ! she secured a maintenance order
against him'. Not being able to pay, be went to
gaol for a year. Yet again she got another main-
tenance order, and he went to gaol for twelve
months more. He emerged from prison in May
last, entered proceedings for divorce, and last
week got a rule nisi.

According to a smart London paper Tas-
mania is frequently referred to as (good phrase!)
“ the Circassia of the colonies,” by reason of the
good looks and splendid figures of its fair
daughters. Comparatively few of them, however,
are lucky enough, like Miss Stephens (the Countess
of Donoughmore), to marry titled folks. They
are in great request as barmaids ; and Melbourne
and Sydney, as well as other Australian capitals,
are constantly competing for their services.”

eldest son of Lady Kintore’s half-brother, and yet

the S.A. West Coast, Caldwell, M.P., and Warren,

audience consisted of members of the expedition,
reporters, and a few settlers who all reclined on
wool-bales and smoked while prayer, praise, and
exhortation continued.

Miss Ackermann, W.C.T.U. lecturess,
tells the story of an Alaska (U.S. A.) woman whom
yl le converted from polyandry to monogamy. The
woman had three husbands, all brothers. One
day she enquired of Miss Ackermann which one
s | ie should keep, so as to become a Christian,
Miss Ackermann told her to decide for herself. A
week later the woman came and said she had
decided. “I enquired,” said Miss Ackermann,
“ which of the husbands she had chosen, and you
wiU be astonished when I tell you which one it
was. Ihe respective ages of the men were 21, 43,
and 44, and the woman chose—the youngest!”
The audience is still scratching its ears in the
endeavour to find out the Ackermann cause for
astonishment.

Judge Williams (Yic.), last week, sen-
tenced a woman to be hanged—and hanged she
will be, probably. Anna Louisa O’Brien seems to
have poisoned off an old farmer named Cornwall,
with whom she lived, at Beulah, Vic., in order to
take up with Piato, a farm labourer. The chosen
drug was arsenic, and the vehicle was soup. By
the way, it was Judge Williams’ father who
sentenced the only woman yet hanged, as far as
we remember, in Victoria.

story:—
A minister in my district was happily married to a wife
very lovely and ladylike, but very deaf. One day they
gave a dinner-party. After dinner they returned to the
drawing-room. Among the guests was a Mr. Hare. He
was standing near the lady whom he had taken in to
dinner, and whom he had entertained delightfully. She,-
not knowing that he was near, remarked to the hostess,
“ IVhat a very agreeable man Mr. Hare is.” The hostess
heard her in a vague, indistinct sort of way, and, thinking
the praise was for her room and not for her company, she
replied: “Yes, and so warm and comfortable on a
winter’s night.”

The Launceston (Tas.) synonym for “going
up King-street ” is “joining the Hunt Club.” Mr.
Hunt, it may be explained, is the Registrar of the
Supreme Court. He is getting members every
week.

Mrs. Dr. Ryder is taken under the wing
of Melbourne missionary meetings, with her
Indian child-wives stories, and draws largely—
with an attraction not wholly dissimilar to that of
the French plays.
» # #
A fashionable lady lately arrived at a tip-
top Melbourne hotel, and interviewed a famous
parrot there. She tickled him under the chin
witli every sugary endearment, but he wouldn’t
talk, till at last he cocked his head knowingly on
one side, and remarked, in a philosophical tone :
“I see you, you !” Worse word than “ bul-
groeity.”
* # *
Governor Jersey, the “Missus” being
away at Samoa, has plunged into dissipation.
His latest outburst is the laying of a Church
foundation-stone at Homebush to the strains of
the National Anathema.
¥ 5^
It has been enacted in Siam that no
prophet is to prophesy locally unless he first
proves his bona fidcs by sitting for lialf-an-hour in
the middle of a large fire. Were this test applied
to the prophets who advertise in Sydney E. News
that they will reveal the future for 2s. 6d., some
interesting results might be arrived at.

that he once performed 23 marriages before break-

last week was the selling up, under distraint, of

At a Primitive Methodist chapel near
Newcastle, N.S.W., the preacher recently dis-
appointed his congregation, whereupon a young
lady of about 19 boldly mounted the pulpit,
announced a text, and wagged her little jaw
extempore for upwards of half-an-hour.
“A former Siberian exile ” declares in the
Contemporary Review that Tolstoi is only a
bloated capitalist after all, and that he piles up
money just as industriously as if he wasn’t a
philanthropist.
The “ Ante-Semitism ” of France has
caused several duels, in one of which a Jewish
Socialist went down before the sword of a
Marquis. Regretting the Jew and Socialist one
can yet almost forgive the Marquis for breaking the
monotonous security of the French duel.
Dear Bulletin, —Your paragraph (August
20) stating that the death-sentence passed on
Joseph James Carter, the Mogill murderer, is the
first pronounced by Mr. Justice Bundey is in-
correct. He tried and sentenced to death Lowen-
thal for shooting his wife at the Adelaide creche,
and, what is more, his report on the case was all
in favour of the death-sentence being carried out.
The fatal bullet fired by Lowenthal, who was re-
prieved, passed through the leg of a baby Mrs.
Lowenthal held in her arms. —Yours, W.

and mental vigor.
FROM SHADOW LAND.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I’m alone with moods and fancies,
And the air is hot and dried,
But a ruddy sparkle dances
In the bottle at my side
As I pass a solemn stricture
On my faults and follies all,
And I gaze at Kendall’s picture
Smiling at me from the wall.
But be sets my thoughts astraying
From the matter I’d in hand,
From my self-inflicted flaying,
To the misty Shadowland,
Where the past is like the present
And there’s neither first nor last
But my journey wasn’t pleasant
Mid the phantoms of the past.
For I saw the wretched poets
Who had sung amid a throng
Of dull clowns that didn’t know its
Sons of sin from sons of Song
Till the grave had darkened o’er them
And they lay among the dead ;
Then the fools fell down before them,
Bringing laurels for the head.
Here’s a happy thought—l struck it
In the gloom of Shadowland—
Ere you kick this earthly bucket,
Here, my brother, is my hand,
If you care to lake a greeting
From a fool you never knew :
Here’s a tumbler to our meeting,
Henry Lawson, here’s to you i
J. LE GAY VEERETON.
