Paramo and the Medicos.
What the Press Says.
TYPOGRAPHICAL TESTIMONY.
THE EDITORS FOR ONCE AGREE.
(New Zealand Times, May 14, 1802.)
The British Medical Journal is very angry
with Lord Onslow. The particular cause of the
Journal’s wrath is that our late Governor has
absolutely dared to testify to the value of remedies
whose composition is not detailed in the British
Pharmacopoeia. The wrath is expressed as
follows ; —“ We see with regret Lord Onslow
shamelessly puffing quack secret remedies by an
advertised letter —as scandalous an abuse of
political position and as discreditable a folly as
lias been for a long time brought under notice.”
Poor Lord Onslow ! The dyspeptic diatribe
above quoted owes its existence, no doubt, to the
fact that Lord Onslow, having found virtue in
some of the Maori herbal remedies prepared by
Mother Aubert, actually had the courage to say
so in print. Why the British Medical Journal
should deem such testimony a high offence, and,
judging by the strength of the language it uses,
an almost criminal misdemeanour, I totally fail to
see, save that the average medical mind is fanatic-
ally opposed to any medical innovation which
does not proceed from recognised red-taped
sources.
’Twas ever thus with the medicos. Almost
every new advance made in medical science has
been bitterly attacked as “quackery” when it
appeared, every new thinker denounced as a mad-
man or worse, and every formula not hall-marked
by the Lancet and British Medical Journal as
a dangerous innovation. Personally, while not
having the pleasure of a personal acquaintance
with either Mother Aubert or Mr. Kempthorne, I
can sympathise with them and Lord Onslow
in seeing the Maori Remedies denounced as
“quack secret remedies.” Only one of those
same “quack remedies” do I know, and that
Karana to wit, which, as “ a real good thing ” for a
man with a liver, I would cordially recommend to
the editor of the B.M.J. He appears to need it
sadly, for the common and domestic and “ recog-
nised ” podophylin has evidently been of no
service to him, otherwise he would never have
penned so spiteful a paragraph. As, however,
the “Maori Remedies” —“quack and secret”
though they be—are reported to be selling like the
proverbial “hot cakes,” neither Mother Aubert
nor Mr. Kempthorne is likely to worry about the
wrath of the British Medical Journal. As for
Lord Onslow, he is at Home, and can fight his
own battle. —“ So*"'tator,” in the New Zealand
Mail.”
The Autumn and Winter Seasons-
As the days become shorter at this period of the
year, with the lesser appearance of the sun, so the
climate becomes more changeable and much colder.
Now is the time when everybody puts on more
clothes to sustain the warmth of the body, but
how few people escape the prevailing coughs and
colds of the autumn and winter seasons, notwith-
standing these precautions.
To all who have suffered from the dire effects of
INFLUENZA, it must be pointed out that they
are much more susceptible to affections of the
throat than when they were in their former good
health.
This has been emphatically proved, and to such
persons a warning is highly necessary.
Marupa
is the Remedy which should be on hand, and
sufferers will always find benefit from its use. No
proprietary medicine has a larger sale in New
Zealand than this now popular remedy, and of its
success there is no doubt, as the testimonials
received acknowledging this are very numerous.
Marupa
is a vegetable preparation, and cannot hurt the
smallest child, but its value in removing irritation
of the lungs and bronchial tubes is far and away
in excess of the price charged for it.
Why be in misery when a bottle of this excellent
combination of herbs will afford joyful relief?
A cold in the head is a very troublesome thing,
but
Marupa
can cure it. All sufferers from chest complaints
are advised to GIVE IT A TRIAL; satisfaction
is bound to result.

is no doubt that vaccination from human lymph may
introduce such diseases as leprosy, cancer, syphilis,
phthisis, scrofula, erysipelas, &c. ; that vaccination with
calf-lymph may infect a person with tuberculosis, cancer,
and cutaneous diseases ; that, in short, vaccination may
not harm all persons, yet may possibly harm many ; and,
anyway, compulsory vaccination is vicious and tyrannical
in principle, since it enforces contamination, compels a
person to breed a disease in bis own body, by means of a
penal law. But all this is not to say that i aocination is not
usually a most valuable preventive against smallpox....

A DOMESTIC CALAMITY
Foremost among what may be termed domestic
calamities is the curse familiarised by the name oi
“ moving house.” The man who can look you i;i
the eye and tell you, without dread of sniffing the
odour of sulphur, that he rather enjoys moving
house, is either a hopeless maniac or a person
desirous of stealing the laurels won by the late
lamented Tom Pepper, whose mendacity was so
pronounced as to incur the marked displeasure of
the King of Hades. The worst of moving house
is that you cannot get away from the dread ordeal.
Your wife insists upon your “getting a day off,”
on the twofold ground that it is your duty to
“look after things,” and “besides, dear, I'm sure
you need a holiday.” And what a p euic that
ho iday turns out to he ! From daybreak the
house is iu confusion. You resolve at the outset
to show how courteous and willing you can be
when the occasion requires, and matters hum
along pleasantly enough until your wife, whom
you entrust to hold on to something while you get
a better grip of it, innocently lets that something
drop with unpleasant emphasis on your favourite
coni. This naturally disturbs the calm senility
of your disposition, and you walk round on one
foot and make observations when no one is listen-
ing. Presently the vanmeii arrive, and with that
instinct peculiar to their kind, proceed to stack
things regardless of classification. Under this
administration chaos reigns in less than no time.
Carpets are wrenched from their resting places,
and blinding clouds of dust assail you at every
turn, while outside fresh annoyances are at hand.
You observe a little crowd of inquisitive young-
sters gathered from the neighbouring streets and
houses who form a semicircle without the garden
gate.
In vain you attempt to disperse them ; your best
efforts are made the subject of merriment, and
yourself the butt of der sion. At length the pro-
cession moves off, the k* ys of the vacated tene-
ment are handed over to the agent, and with a
mental resolve that no mortal agency will ever
induce you to move house again, you set out to
encounter fresh trials of endurance in the new
domicile. It is only when moving house that a
man makes the discovery that his domestic stock-
in-trade comprises a variety of impediments that
he might very comfortably get along without, and
it is also on such occasions, when he finds himself
suffering at one and the same lime from a strained
back, a bruised thumb, and a throbbing headache,
that ho blesses the day when so perfect an
antidote to pain as St. Jacobs Oil was discovered,
its value as a remedial and curative agent attested
by actual and incontrovertible results.

Apropos the cabled statement that Labou-
cherc was omitted from the new Gladstone
Ministry because Queen Victoria didn’t like him.
None of the dailies have alluded in their obituary
otices to the. real cause of the extinction of
‘‘Bob Lowe’s ” political career. When Beacons-
Uyirl introduced his Royal Titles Bill, Lowe,
who opposed it, divulged the fact that Queen
Victoria had tried to induce the Liberal Cabinet
to make her Empress, but had signally failed.
Lowe’s speech gave mortal offence to the Court,
and the Queen declared she would never again
receive him as a Minister. To salve his feelings,
Gladstone made him a lord, and thus finished him.
We have received from a Brisbane photo-
grapher what is evidently intended to be a crush-
ing refutation of the theory that the Queensland
Kanaka business is virtually slavery. It consists
of a photo, entitled “ Queen street in 1894,” and
represents a Kanaka in an eye-glass, gloves, long
cuffs, a huge bell-topper, and top boots, meeting
another Kanaka in a loud check suit, an eye-glass,
a supercilious smile, and an umbrella. The former
individual is accompanied by a dusky lady in a
bonnet, a pair of stays, frilled continuations, and
a lace parasol; and the latter by another dusky
lady iu a divided skirt, a lace parasol, and an
affable grin, and a murky infant in a kilt, and
carrying a pot of jam. Attached is the inscrip-
tion, “Is this slavery ?” Apparently it isn’t. As
far as The Bulletin can make out —after viewing
the lady in the garment before alluded to, and
making due allowance for the obvious want of
pants in the case of one gentleman—it is only
plain indecency.

SPORTING NOTIONS'
N. R. D. Bond, who died lately, aped 05,
had three stages in his turf career—rider, writer and club-
secretary. Even in his second stage few people were
aware that he had been a steeplechaser almost of Gordon’s
fame, for he didn’t look it, or speak it, at all. Quiet
Hondo, with his head screwed on one side like a prize
canary. No man’s enemy, not even his own. Bond in-
vented the numbered saddle-cloth for racing. The Earl
of Drogheda, visiting Australia, was so struck with it that
he introduced it in Ireland, and certainly it will become
universal all over England. As tipsters, Bond and Augur
Chapman were equally bad ; each made big hits on which,
so to speak, they lived for years. Bond struck four
winners out of five races one V.R.C. day, early in his
writing stage, jusc as Chapman made a prodigious spot -
stroke score on the eve of his vault to the Australasian ;
and another fellow called “Warwick” once did the four-
out-of-five trick—a thing which the tipster can hardly
live down, however he may degenerate. But the tipster
declines as he waxes fat and leaves off touting.
* * *
Memmott v. Evans, billiard-match, Melbourne,
brings out the question whether immense breaks are not
made simply by the chalk instead of the ball, the chalk
making a roadway, as it were.
* * #
The idea that “something had been done ” to
the table on which Memmott scored his big screw-back
break is only pardonable for the reason that the perform-
ance was so very extraordinary. It seems absurd to
suppose that the cloth could be specially “ faked ” with
a view to one particular stroke, and as Evans could find
no more “ depression ” than admitted of the reasonable
explanation that the red ball bad done it he will have to
persuade himself that he was fairly beaten bv a freak of
skill on Memmott’s part. The pace at which the ball
had to be potted puts the genuineness of the feat beyond
question by unbiassed minds.
• • •
Since the Dog Show at Melbourne Exhibition
closed, the dailies are doing a roaring trade in “ ads.” for
lost dogs. N.B.—There are five alligators in the Exhibi-
tion building.
* * *
The big turf-men denounce pony-racing for its
bare-faced swindles. The only difference between it and
the “legitimate ” game seems to be that, in the former
case, the sums, like the nags, are smaller. The wholesale
man always despises the retailer.

Maori Warbrick—the W. G. Grace of football
who captained the last Queensland team in N.S.W., has
been laid out senseKss some score of times, has had his
collar-bone broken twice, his leg broken once, and his
shin splintered.

out cf every kind of “ sport,” so-called, of which book-

At Palo-Alto, the celebrated American breeding
farm, they have started teaching several blue-blood
thoroughbreds to trot from infancy. The people at Palo-
Alto say it is on the cards they will more than hold their
own with trotting-bred stock.

Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent’s yarn
about the card-sharper doing (or trying to do) “ the razor
trick ” with a “ mug ” is not bad. Worst of it is that it’s
a lie. I will bet anyone in Australia or anywhere else £lO
he cannot cut a full pack of M cards “ clean through
the middle at one cut” with a razor—even with the
deceased Deeming’s razor.—Yours, Tvn Kbtil.
* • #
Dear Bulletin,—We had a tug-of-war last week
in this God-forsaken bush township, and it ended badly.
I don’t think the tug-of-war is the thing it is cracked up
to be. There were twelve men on either side, and it
would have been a pretty even thing but for a new-chum
German, over six feet high, that Jimmy, the publican,
roped-in at the last moment. He overset all the calcu-
lations. Everybody wanted to bet on the German’s crowd,
and Bill, the other publican, took all the odds that were
going till he stood to lose over £2OO. The tug
came off in the roadway opposite Jimmy’s pub., and
everybody was there. Just as the men got down
to business and the rope was stretched tight a shabby-
looking stranger ranged up alongside the big German,
and said something in the Teutonic language and in a
gentle undertone. The German was so wildly surprised
that he let go the rope, and the other side gained a foot
before he got hold again. Then the stranger said some-
thing else, and the Teuton began to foam at the mouth,
and shifted round uneasily, and while he was shifting
nearly eighteen inches more were lost. At this point,
we began to suspect something wrong, and Jimmy
the publican seized the stranger by the collar, anil
dragged him away, but while he was being shifted
he yelled out another mass of strange language, and
right there the German let go the rope altogether, and
with a wild shriek of “ mein frail 1” he ran for his home a
mile and a half away. The tug lasted just three seconds
after that, and all our money was scooped in by Bill, the
other publican, with a hideous grin on his countenance.
We are all cleaned out. It turned out that the stranger
had been telling the simple-minded German that his
infant had a fit at home and was choking to death, and
that his house was on fire, and his eldest son had been
trodden on by a bullock. When he heard these things
Fritz made superhuman efforts to finish the tug-of-war so
ns to get away and put out the fire, and rescue the baby,
and pick up the damaged son ; but when the other man
told him that his wife had fallen into the tank and that
his sister was being murdered by a sundowner, he let
everything go and ran for it. I think this sort of thing
ought to be put down.—Yours, &c., Titus Salt.

A horse yarn from up South. Two owners, X
and Z, had horses entered for the big Handicap, and each
feared the other’s horse only. After a little shuffling
they agreed to do a trial over the distance, weights up
and both to back the better horse. After the horse had
gone about two furlongs, X’s horse fell into the rear and
pulled up dead lame ; the other finished excellently well,
and Z, condoling with his unfortunate friend, hurried in
and backed his horse down to oto 4. When the actual
race came off the lame (?) nag went to the post and,
beating his field at the distance, won as he liked, his
owner netting a nice sum from the books, and the
majority of the totalisator money. How was it done ?
Well, get a piece of fine cafgnt, and brushing the hair up
just above the fetlock tie the catgut firmly round the
joint and brush the hair down again. It defies detection.
When the pressure through galloping comes on the
sinews the horse soon feels the pain, and there j’ou are !
A warm fomentation takes away any ill effects. That
this happened is a very solemn fact.

privately, when, after a terrible battle, M‘Carthy knocked

Dear Bulletin,—The Leichhardt (Sydney)
Rowing Club has written the Victorian Rowing Associa-
tion offering to send a crew to compete in the Inter-
colonial Champion Fours, to bo rowed in Melbourne in
October. The proposed four do not altogether fit into
the definition of an amateur under Victorian rules, but if
this difficulty is got over it is asked that the entry may be
received as a N.S.W. Champion Four. Whatever the
V.R.A. may do about accepting the entry, a crew taken
from a single club, and that club outside the pale of the
N.S.W. Association, cannot by any stretch of definitions
ha ranked as a champion four. In connection with the
bona-fide and manual labour trouble the writer cannot see
anything to prevent the manual labour clubs coming
under fie present Association, on a separate footing, but
enter into racing competition with the bona-fide amateur
they must not. The writer is not penning these lines on
the idea that a man who carts bricks or wields a sledge is
not (it to row in the same boat as a man who handles a
quill, but simply on the ground that all chance of the
av erage (piilldriver gaining a pot would be at an end.
And, under such circumstances, is it reasonable to expect
the clubs who have worked under difficulties for years in
the interests of non-manual rowing, now that all" is plain
sailing, to step out and allow the manual-labour cuckoos to
usurp their nests ? Not much.—W.

Re the proposal to send Redleaf) to England :
The long sea-voyage may have much injured the late
James White’s youngsters, which had to stand on deck
for several weeks. Instead of the long voyage, the 'Frisco
route should be adopted, and the journey could be broken
at Auckland, Honolulu, and through the States, so that
the horse need never be more than a week at sea. Ring-
master travelled from India only to England.

Dear Bulletin, — Concerning your par. re Th e
Agent: That horse was originally purchased out of th e
Mt. Gambier Pound, and found its way to Narracoort 6
(S.A.), where he was owned as Traduoer by A. Hinckley,
and stood as an entire, but with poor success. J. £)■
Furlonge, then a travelling agent for the A.M.P. Society,
wanted a horse for saddle or buggy, and admired the
fat, glossy little chestnut, and gave Hinckley a trifle over
£20 for him. Furlonge drove him to Mt. Gambier, and it
was at Fred Harris’ (Mac.’s Hotel) that Sandy Miller, who
was training there at the time, persuaded his owner to
let him give the horse a spin. Miller’s good opinion was
borne out by his winning his first three races at Border
in the mud. His career in Western Victoria was more
brilliant than his owner’s, and after that all Australia
knows his repord. These facts can be verified by hosts of
your readers, and may serve to contradict a lot of silly
yarns I have heard about this truly great little horse.
He was named “The Agent” after his then owner’s
occupation.—Yours truly, S.A., Boorooma, N.S.W., via
Brewarrina.

ALWAYS SECOND.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Are the bitters of life being reckoned ?
I can name, in one trial, the worst:
’Tis the horse that comes in a “ good second,”
But has never the luck to be “ first.”
By elusive, delusive Hope, beckoned,
Still the owner will back the accurst,
Pity prophets who er3', “ He’ll be second,”
Put a pot on the chance, “ He’ll be first! ”
As with horse, so with man, ruin’s reckoned
By the failures that (bus are rehearsed :
“ Only lost ‘by a pip ! ’ He was second ;
With a humbug, called ‘Thrift,’ always first.”

Dear Bulletin, —You give the so-called
Australian Institute of Journalists a push up
every now and again. Why is this thus? What
has the loud-sounding Institute got to say for
itself? David Syme and several of the whip-hand
gentlemen figure largely on its list, and it starts
out to smash and confound the libel-law. Very
good, but the said libel-law is chiefly the bosses
funeral, after all. Why is no effort made to stop
the cheap and nasty English weeklies that have
suffocated so many local productions ? The
freelances of Melbourne regard the Institute as
a daily-paperman’s concern, and ate “not in
it.”—Yours, Snell.
A year or two ago the billet of postmistress
fell vacant in a South Queensland township, and
the advent of the lady who now holds the position
is still remembered with horror by the magistrate
before whom she signed the customary declara
tioh. She was a massive person with a deter-
mined countenance, and had evidently been
accustomed to write her signature on a sheet of
bark with a fire-stick. When the document was
put befoi’e her she remarked that she didn’t sec
how she was to get her autograph into that little
bit of a thing, and then she seized the pen with an
air of desperate resolution, drove it into the ink-
bottle, and started. Her Christian name spread
right across the paper, and then her surname
slopped over on to the table and spread all over
it, and started to climb up the wall, and when
she had got as high up as she could
reach she had still half-a-dozen hieroglyphs left.
But just then the P.M. stopped her, and said that
the signature wasn’t legal if any of it was con-
structed out of the province, and the part of it
that spread across the border couldn’t be taken
into consideration, and moreover, he pointed out,
very few civil Servants could read their own signa-
tures, and none of them ever expected to be able
to read those of other people, therefore he
suggested that the lady should go away quietly,
and he would submit that portion of her autograph
which was on the paper, and if she had no objec-
tion they would let the bit that was on the_ table
and the wall slide. They let it slide accordingly,
and the lady got the appointment and holds it to
this day.
The singular death of an elderly lady from,
it is stated, suffocation, but more certainly from
fright, which occurred at Katoomba (N.S.W.)
last week is the last episode in a sequence of
tragedies. Married while in her early teens, her
first husband was lost in the Dunbar. Again
marrying—this time the son of one of the few who
justly won distinction by public work in N.S.W.
—her second husband also came to his end sud-
denly. While his and his wife’s fortunes together
totted up nearly a hundred thousand he was
under the delusion that lie was poverty-stricken,
and committed suicide in and from that belief.
She, after an interval of widowhood, married once
more, but the dread of poverty induced her to
enter into a most extraordinary arrangement with
a person well known in political and financial
circles. This was on the basis of an annuity,
during her own life, in consideration of which she
made over the fee of valuable Sydney property,
the transaction now resulting in a tremendous
profit for the financier. As if this was not enough
to distinguish the woman’s life—or her death
from the ordinary, it may be added that a
deaf mute in Glasgow (in no way con-
nected with her', at present living in poverty,
comes into possession of a life-interest in Bris-
bane property worth .£25,000, and that with
her passes into the grave the key to one of the
hushed-up mysteries of Sydney society a genera-
tion ago. She gave “ Brice Waning ” the matter
for a strongly dramatic story—which he is now
free to tell. She died intestate, after making—
and destroying—a score of wills.
Sir Henry Parkes has announced that he
is engaged upon a work entitled “Eminent Aus-
tralians.” It lias been Sir Henry’s fortune, as he
has often told us, to have been brought into con-
tact with every man of eminence who has strutted
his hour upon the stage of Australian history for
the last four decades. And, we may add, it was
his misfortune to have come at one time or another
under the lash of every one of them. It will be
interesting to notice how he deals with his old
opponents in regard to whom he has at least this
satisfaction, that he has outlived them all and is
still well, vide birth-notices in the press.
How, for instance, will he treat Dailey, who once
said of one of his insolvencies that if Parkes lived
much longer he would rule over a nation not of
friends or electors but of creditors, and followed
this neat piece of satire up by remarking of the
Parkes schedule that it read “like Leporello’s
catalogue of the amours of his wicked master, or
like a parody upon the character of that immortal
Churchill who could take with the same grace and
the same ingratitude half a million livres from his
Imperial Majesty of France and twopence half-
penny from a British drummer-boy?” And
what will he say of Darvall, the English
aristocrat, who, on one memorable occasion,
upon the Maitland hustings, turned back his
superfine cuffs with the remark, “And now I
have to deal with a dirty subject ’’—the subject
alluded to being the identical Parkes who is now
engaged in begetting young Parkeses and writing
about Darvall aforesaid ? And Deniehy—who
described Parkes sitting in the library at Parlia-
ment House constructing political schemes, “quite
as intricate but not half so clean as those which
used to engage his attention in his little turner’s
shop in Hunter-street” —what of him? How
often did William Forster lash the hermit of Bal-
main with that iron flail of invective and satire
which he knew so well how to wield ! Is Forster
to be included in the “ Heminent Horsetralians ”?
And will Parkes also include Sir Alfred Stephen,
whose memorable remarks, &c. &c. ? Anyone
desirous of prompt and imperishable fame should
produce a companion volume to Parkes’ which
might be called “ Eminent Australians upon
Parkes,” For the latter has at least the credit of
having provoked by far the best and bitterest
things that eminent Australians have said or
written, and a collection of these would be much
more delicious reading than the volume at present
gestating at Hampton Villa is at all likely to
prove. Parkes may treat of Australian politicians
well enough if he can conquer that insane and
overweening vanity and jedSousy which prompts
him to regard himself as first and the rest no-
where ; but when he comes to deal with pro-
fessors of belles-lettres like Dailey and Deniehy
and Forster, he will fail miserably. The literary
instinct he never had, and as a critic of literary
character he is as impossible as he is as a poet.
Literature has tried him and ejected him as
something foreign, unpleasant, and utterly
indigestible.
Dear Bulletin, —A recent issue of Sydney
E. News tells all about “the smallest, simplest,
and best-protected post-office in the world . . .
a small painted keg or cask • . . chained to
the rocks of the extreme cape, in a manner so that
it floats free, opposite Terra del Fuego,” in the
Magellan Straits. “Each passing ship sends a
boat to take letters out and put others in, &c.,
&c. Is this correct? I have been through
Magellan Straits several times, both as chief officer
and commander, having to land mails each trip at
Sandy Point, a Chilian settlement. I never heard
of this wonderful mail-cask chained to rocks.
Such a fixture would be impossible at Cape Pillar.
Cape Virgins has no settlement, I think ; it had
none in the sixties. I know of no other cape there.
—Yours, Shellback.
Dear Bulletin, —Can anyone explain why
Adam Lindsay Gordon made such a mess of Ins
classical allusions ? Take the following instances
(from fourth edition of his poems) -.—Page 2, we
find, “Polyrena.” This is a rasper, as the name
is so well known to lower-school boys. Page
“Mors (jratam, vita brevis.” This is a howler’
Page 157, “caro pejus.” Page 158, “nunc s
unquam, nunc aut nunquam.” Surely not Latin .
Page 160, “ caro fortius. Page 209, “ Aphrodite ”
rhymes with “light.” This is a very sore afflic-
tion. lam rather ashamed to pose an advocatus
Diaboli, for some of Gordon’s things are so good
that one might pardon these slips; but I have
some hope that his friends may explain that he
never saw these poems in book-form, while he
might not have been able to correct errors when
they appeared in newspapers. —W. P. Howlett,
Pahiatua, Maoriland.

The Jackson Party’s Dodge.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Old Bluegum had the shanty at the ballast-getters’
camp—
The usual slie-oak depot of the usual navvy stamp ;
’Twas built of tattered calico and canvas and old
sacks,
And boxes held together with fencing-wire and
tacks.
The beer was served in pannikins—imperial pints,
I mean;
The rum was forty overproof, but tempered with
benzine;
Old Bluegum kept no other stuffs, and not too
much of those.
For “traps” were apt to come along, and traps
were Bluegum’s foes.
They had a most unpleasant way, these trooper
fellows did.
Of getting shanty-keepers at the Court fined
“ thirty quid,”
And if he saw them coming, and rolled it out,
perhaps
He’d find his hard-earned tangle would be soaked
up by the chaps.
Now, business had been languishing, and twice
had he been fined,
And Bluegum, then, to “make a punch ” applied
his thoughtful mind ;
Things had been mighty slow with us since Bricky
Welsh’s fight.
So Bluegum’s proposition was received with much
delight:
“You coves,” he said, “from Jackson’s don’t
fight with Brown’s no more,
You don’t seem on for poker, and you're not
inclined for gore,
I'll tell you how to make things hum-we'll have
a tug-of-war !”
Now, “Jackson’s” was a boarding-house the
engine-push fed there—
A lot of stuck-up puppies, tco, those swagg’ring
oil-rags were;
And if there was one joy we craved, we chaps who
stayed with Brown,
’Twas giving Jackson’s engine push a real good
taking-down—
From Bill who drove the loco., a six-foot hulking
lout,
To Jim the kid, who greased the wheels, and
cleaned the donkey out.
We sent a challenge, therefore, to them, saying—
“ Are you game,
You hungry-looking objects who disgrace old
Jackson’s name,
To pull us for a pound a man, with twenty men
a-side ?”
And Bill, the loco.-driver, then with brevity
replied—
“ We’re on —but when we’ve done with you, you
coves’ll wish you’d died !”
’Twas down upon the railway-line we went to try
our strength,
We’d cleared away the ballast about forty sleepers’
length
To get a good stiff purchase to brace our feet
against—
We wanted it, I tell you, when the whole of us
commenced
To lie down to the tussle, at the word of Jack the
Png.,
And forty fellows started on that most heait-
rending tug!
For nearly seven minutes from the time we set our
sails,
The sleepers cracked, the fish-plates bent—we
thought we’d curl the rails !
With muscles up on stiffened arms, and glaring
eyeballs, then,
A sight to be remembered long was that three ton
of men !
An inch we gained—an inch we lost—another inch
got back
There hasn’t any trouble there ’bout hauling in
the slack !
But Billy Brown, our captain, said: “They’ll
crack up all to pot,
They haven’t got the bottom that you quarrymen
have got;
For putting toughness in a man, the best concern
of all’s
The twenty-one pound hammer that you use to
quarry spawls.”
And soon there wasn’t half a doubt about our
captain’s tip,
We gained an inch —a foot—two feet! we had
them on the whip !
Another foot! we raised a yell! we had ’em fairly
done !
That yell was just what cooked us—if we’d held
it, we’d have won!
For hardly had we settled down to finish with a
“ whoosh !”
Than seven devils seemed to hold that engine-
driving push !
We tugged like twenty bullock-teams, artistically
cussed,
We strained at that infernal rope until wo nearly
bust.
Our tongues hung out about a foot, we hadn’t time
to swear,
For we were licked by Jackson's push before we
were aware !
They ran us off like infants, and, as Bill the
driver’d said,
We wished, with one united wish, the lot of us
were dead !
We paid the stakes to Jackson's crowd, but I
really didn't think
They’d go straight off to 1 Ir.cgum’s and not ask
us to drink !
They did ! (he thieving, sn a' i ;g crus, and added
to their crime
By sing’ng'out—“We'll dr'nk yv r health and
wish you luck next tin e !’
Then gloo nily we loafed arum I, and not a fellow
spoke
(Wo would have gone to Bluegum's, 100, hut
every man was broke ;
The pound a man we could have stood, hut that
was not enough,
For every blessed fool of us had bet, and lost, his
stuff),
Till all at one? old Brown came out with a
tremc nclous swear.
And shook his fists, and pranced around, and tore
out chunks of hair.
And pointed to the rope and said, “Gehenna!
strike me blind !
D’ye see the darned infernal donkea-nnjinc hitched
behind ! /”
We looked, and not a man could speak for a
minute, say, or more,
And then the very rope curled up to hear the way
we swore !
For Jackson’s end man, when we yelled, had let
go at the back
And round the donkey-engine drum had wound
six feet of slack,
And then had set her going—she was nine horse-
power full
And wo had nearly killed ourselves in trying that
to pull !
Said Brown : “ We’ll go to Bluegum’s, and we’ll
scatter round some wool! ”
But when wo got to Bluegum’s, where those
scoundrels should have been,
Two traps were there, a lookin’ round, and Blue-
gum wasn’t in ;
He’d seen the troopers coming, and had cleared off
to the bush,
And then his beer was collared by that thievin’
Jackson push !
They’d rolled it to old Jackson’s tent, and straight-
way put it down,
They’d got as drunk as Chloe, without spendin’
half-a-crown !
And when we tried to slaughter ’em , ’twas we who
<'jot done brown !
A. CHEE.

The City Minins’ Man.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Spot his highly-polished “ topper,” note his patter
glib and proper, and the neatness of his flogger,
and his linen spick and span ;
See the cable at his pocket, and the twenty-ounces
locket, he’s the model and the picture of a
Melbourne mining man.
He’s a swell—observe his bingey-and there’s
notiiing dull or dingy ’bout his dainty patent-
leathers and the di’monds in Ins shirt ;
He’s a clubman, and ho gailj does the Block in
numbers daily, he's a mining man who never
moved a shovelful of dirt.
’Tis this party, ’cute and clever, whom admiring
papers ever love to speak of with effusion, as
the speculator bold
Who has risked his time and treasure, and his
5 comfort, in a measure, just to boom the droop-
ing industry of burrowing for gold.
At a spread or christ’ning function, speakers dwell
with warmth and unction on the enterprise
and energy this “ miner ” docli display,
And each rapturous oration proves his benefited
nation owes the gent, a debt of gratitude it
never can defray.
Then the money he’s invested gives the journals
interested further chances to enlarge upon his
virtue and renown,
And hurl curses at tire workers, at the strikers
and the “shirkers,” whose actions tend to
raise his risks and keep his profits down.
Strange, they never speak at feeds or at their
guzzles, of the deeds, or of the losses of the
men who’ve only work to throw away,
And the pluck that they exhibit, who are working
on a “ tribit ” that has given barely tucker for
a twelve-month and a day.
Tom and Dick may work like asses in the water
and the gases, whilst their babes are often
hungry, and their wives grow weak and thin,
Then, suppose they chance to strike it, well, the
company doesn’t like it, and it bounces them
on law-points which the bosses always win.
’Tain’t the sweating men —poor devils ! —down in
stifling lower levels, getting loaded full of
poison or the miner's pet disease,
That the rags feel interesting; life and limb are
they investing, but the nation doesn’t seem to
owe a penny piece to these.
No, the mining man they bow to and the miner
they kow-tow to is the mining man of Collin
street, who never gripped a drill.
And who wouldn’t know the crosscuts from a
handsaw, but, of course, cuts quite the fattest
kind of figure as he swaggers on his skill.
He’s the man who does the lying, and the selling,
and the buying, and who raves about his
rights—those rights he cherishes so well ;
And who handles all the treasure, and enjoys
abundant leisure his rewards for having
noodles on the bifurcated shell.
Let those gentlemen repining o’er the awful cost
of mining, ask the gaudy Melbourne miner
why this painful thing is thn»,
And the party who announces that the cost of
every ounce is so much greater than its value,
go to him and raise a fuss.
He’d no great amount to start with, now quite
cheerfully he’ll part with many hundreds for
the razzle his ambitious wife is giving;
And below, in slopes and faces, scores of workers
take their places to provide this gilded loafer
with a poor excuse for living.
EDWARD DYSON.

Monthly, Critic, Journal of Australasia,
Melbourne Review, Once a Month, Victorian

Dear Bulletin,“Neither of your corres-
pondents tells the whole story of Blind Tom, the
woodcutter of Lefroy. Ht; had his back broken
in five places, and one ear b'.tten off trying to
capture the Dora Dora blacks years ago. They
were young men at that time, too. lie still goes
out, selects his fells it, makes some fiddles
and pianos and tiling and comes home to supper
at (i. He uses an American axe.—Boiling Billy.
The article on the late police-magistrate
Pater in S. A. Register— written evidently by
Rowland Rees, ex-M.P.—contains an occasional
piece of fact thrown in with obituary statements.
Here is one : “ He hated cant and that Pharisaism
which his and my experience teach secure position
and power here as elsewhere.” In Adelaide,
Chief Secretary Cockbnrn who really holds re-
markably enlightened views about religion—gets
np at a Salvation Army meeting and prays that
Cod may bless “ the work.”
One more nigger has been duly scragged
by the strong arm of the law at Gerald ton (W.A.)
on a charge of murder. A settler named Waldeck
had lost, or said he had lost, some sheep, and with-
out any ceremony he seized the nearest native by
the hair and tied him up to a tree, whether with
a view of committing a brutal assault upon him,
or shooting him, or keeping him prisoner without
law or evidence for an indefinite time, doesn’t
appear. Also, as Waldeck is dead now this point
will never be cleared up. During the white man’s
absence the aboriginal escaped, and discovering
this fact Waldeck mounted his horse and went in
pursuit. He presently overtook the heathen, and
deliberately tired at him four times, and then
the black individual (much the better shot of
the two) threw a spar by way of response,
and Waldeck was nev .of any more use. The
jury hesitated to convict at first, on the ground
that the white man being the aggressor, the
aboriginal was probably using his weapon in self-
defence, but the court pointed out that Waldeck
must have either fired on one side or over the
nigger’s her 1, else he would hare hit him, and under
these conditions the other fellow was acting un-
justifiably. Therefore, no nigger is allowed to
shoot back in W.A. unless he has been killed in
the first place; if ho deends himself before he is
killed he’ll be h.fbgod, and if he does it after he is
killed he’ll naturally be dead. Sentence of death
by hanging, as usual, the Lord being requested to
have mercy on a small vboriginal’s soul. This is
no burlesque: our narrative is based on the evi-
dence as solemnly reported in the W.A. papers.
